2.45        Profitabilitv glements.   At present, PBDAC derives its revenues
primarily from lending, to a lesser extent from input trading and to a minor
extent from banking services and fees. Interest income rose from 61 per cent of
total revenues in FY 1988 to 72 per cent of total revenues in FY 1992, while
input trading provided 23 per cent of total revenues in FY 1992 compared to 28
per cent in FY 1988. Other revenues, including fees and other income, made up
the remaining 5 per cent of the total revenues in FY 1992.
2.46        The faster growth of interest expenses has largely contributed to the
decline in earnings in 1992. Measures for improving PBDAC's profitability are
incorporated into the proposed medium term Business Plan (Annex 2, Part B), which
include steps for reducing personnel costs and dependence on commercial bank
borrowings, greater mobilization of resources, and further diversification of
loan portfolio. These measures would be supported under the project, as well as
under projects supported by other donors, notably USAID.



- 21 -
2.47        Critical Link in PBDAC's Profit Plannino.   Village banks are a
critical link in the PBDAC system. A village bank in considered viable when it
attains a level of loan business providing it an income level which would enable
it to meet not only all operating expenses but also help it build a reasonable
level of profit over a period of time. Analyzing the experience of a typical
village bank, with average annual administration costs of around LB 94,000, the
break-even point of amount of loans to be advanced for an average branch would
be at LB 2 million. This level of loan business at LB 2 million per annum would
only enable a village bank attain break-even level of self-sufficiency. It is
important that the village bank sets a higher target if it is to earn profits to
build up improved and efficient credit delivery, supervision, deposit
mobilization, and the required bad debt and other reserves.
PBDAC's Medium-term Strateev
2.48        The senior management of PBDAC recognizes that its operations in the
medium-term need considerable attention with particular reference to ensuring its
financial viability. Accordingly, a medium-term Business Plan has been prepared
by PBDAC, which outlines the strategy for developing the bank into an effective
rural financial intermediary (Annex 2, Part B). The Plan identifies measures to
strengthen organization and management of the institution, measures for effective
mobilization of rural savings, reduction of staff costs, and adoption of new
systems and procedures in order to improve operational efficiency and
competitiveness.   These changes would be accompanied by appropriate human
resources development, including managerial, technical, and support staff, to
develop a full-fledged service operation.   Finally, financial planning and
continual monitoring of financial performance are to be emphasizeds development
of financial management expertise, definition of analytical tools and management
reports, and appropriate use of information systems are important elements in
this strategy.
2.49        The package of measures outlined in the PBDAC's medium-term Business
Plan specifies the bank's strategy to improve its profitability and
competitiveness through cutback of unprofitable services and activities,
introduction of new services and expansion of existing more profitable services,
and better cost control. Assurances were obtained at necotiations that PBDAC
would Provide the Bank not later than March 31 of each year with a Business Plan.
with annual taroets to be achieved durina the following years the Plan will
include oroiections for the comina five years IDara. 5.03id)1.  In addition, the
Business Plan will provide details of the PBDAC's operations, including: (i)
lending targets; (ii) funding needs and resource mobilization strategy; (iii)
detailed financial statements; (iv) strategy for human resources management,
including assessment of manpower and training needs; (v) management of physical
resources; and (vi) framework for improving financial viability of the bank and
for formulating operating policies and procedures.
2.50        Financial projections, including ass..ptions, are included as part
of the Business Plan in Annex 2, Part B.  Projected growth rates have been
expressed in nominal terms. Historically, the volume of PBDAC'S lending has been
limited by the availability of funds rather than demand.   Nevertheless,
projections have been made of resource requirements and resource availabilities
based on past trends. On this basis and assuming zero per cent real growth rate,
PBDAC projects an increase in deposits by LB 593 million during the period FYs



- 22 -
1993-97; commercial bank borrowings would increase marginally and its share would
decline by LE 13 million over the same period, declining from 29 per cent to 19
per cent of the loan portfolio.  Personnel costs account for a substantial
portion of PBDAC's operating costs, and the non-implementation of the early
retirement program would lead to increased costs in the future. However, results
of sensitivity analysis done indicate that PBDAC would remain financially viable
over the medium term aven if the early retirement program is not implemented.
Projections also show that P8DAC would be able to achieve its goal of utilizing
outside borrowings particularly from commercial banks (LE 1.2 billion) to finance
mainly seasonal crop production and short-term investment loans (LE 2.0 billion).
Provisions for possible loan losses as a proportion of total loan outstanding are
projected to increase steadily from the current 8.8 per cent in FY 1992 to 12 per
cent in FY 1997. Income set aside to meet this contingency would be able to
absorb these losses even if the recovery rate is assumed to fall to 88 per cent,
an unlikely event in the light of better loan appraisal and improved recovery
measures proposed under the project. Even under this conservative scenario, the
projections reveal an increasing size of net profit before taxee.
1II. THE PROJECT
A. Background
3.01        The major issues facing the sector at this stage are: more efficient
use of water and inputs; modernization of farm practices to improve their
efficiency; timeliness and cost effectiveness in field operations;  enhancement
of productivity in reclaimed lands; and uss of credit as a means to promote
private sector involvement in providing improved technology and services in
production, processing and marketing of agricultural products.   The project
responds to these needs.
3.02        Generation of new technology, as well as adaptation and dissemination
of available technology, is done mainly by Government agencies, and, to a lesser
extent, the academia; private sector involvement in these activities is limited.
Dissemination of technology has remained tenuous, largely because of weak
linkages between research and extension services. Development of small- and
medium-scale agro-based enterprises and post-harvest processing and marketing
activities has received only limited assistance until now. More attention to
these activities will open up new off-farm employment opportunities and increase
household incomes.
B. Rationale for Bank Involvement
3.03        The Government's role in the agricultural sector is now being limited
to a few key areas of providing major irrigation and drainage infrastructure;
adopting and implementing appropriate legislation and regulation regarding land
ownership and tenure, health and safety issues; provision of agricultural
research and extension services; and collection and dissemination of technical
and economic data relating to the sector. Private enterprise has been given a
critical role in marketing new production and processing technology and in
developing markets for products. The improved policy environment which now
exists in Egypt, with particular reference to the agricultural sector, is
conducive to profitable private investment.   The project would buttress and
strengthen these developments.



- 23 -
3.04        The project is in conformity with the Bank's country assistance
strategy as described in the Memorandum of the President (Report No. P-6203-EGT).
It is designed to support private sector development in the rural areas as well
as increase agricultural and rural incomes by improving access to investment
capital and technology for small- and medium-scale rural operations.  Rural
banking plays a critical role in this respect. The project also aims to promote
efficiency and competition in the rural credit market and is a part of reforms
in Egypt's financial sector. PBDAC's efforts to restructure and streamline its
organization under the project represents a pilot effort at administrative reform
and improved public sector management both of which are important to the Bank's
strategy in Egypt.
C. Proiect Conaent
3.05        The project concept is based ons (i) the introduction of integrated
farming packages, which, among other things, enable farmers to apply timely
cultural practices, including row planting, harvesting, and land levelling; and,
(ii) investments to reduce post-harvest losses and improve marketing services.
With the removal of controls on pricing and distribution of inputs and outputs,
the agricultural economy has become market-directed and the farming system is
becoming   commercially-oriented.       The   project   would   promote   such
commercialization. The ongoing liberalization of the financial sector, combined
with the liberalization of trade and investment regulations, provides a favorable
climate for the banking system to play its role of supporting rural entrepreneurs
to utilize modern technology, inputs and systems for advancing their production,
processing and marketing activities.
3.06        Egypt has made steady progress in technology development and use of
improved seed and planting materials, row seeding, integrated pest management in
cotton and vegetable production, custom-hire machinery services for harvesting
and threshing of grains, balanced feed diets for poultry, and artificial
insemination for cattle. However, there is considerable scope to expand the
application of these practices. The project concept attempts to capture the
dynamic nature of technology development. Through on-farm demonstrations, the
SMSs would generate demand for new types of machinery and equipment; and, through
provision of credit, traders, manufacturers and custom hire operators would be
enabled to meet the demand. Unlike in the past, the promotion of these improved
practices will occur without resort to subsidies, under free market conditions.
This poses a challenge not only to the public institutions, but also to the
private sector traders, the farming community and the banking sector. By using
credit as a vehicle for promoting use of improved technology and management
practices, the project seeks to open new opportunities for investment and bring
about a change in the system.
3.07        There are abundant opportunities for mechanization in Egyptian
agriculture. Use of laser land levelling has considerable appeal to farmers as
it leads to more efficient water use, better plant stands and improved yields.
Crop husbandry practices, such as weeding, transplanting, fertilizing, etc., lend
themselves to effective secondary mechanization and better use of available
labor. At present, the turn-around time between crops takes up to 20 per cent
of the growing season, which, in turn, results in late planting and drop in
yields: mechanization would help ameliorate this problem. Similarly, inadequate
processing and marketing services have become a bottleneck in the growth of
agriculture.   Sizable gains made in wheat, rice and maize production have
generated demand for investment in milling, storage, and transport activities.
Similar opportunities exist in dairy, meat, and horticultural sub-sectors. So



- 24 -
far, with the exception of a few investments made under externally-financed
operations, these types of projects have not received much support from the
banking community. Assistance to these activities would be encouraged under the
project.
3.08        The banking system has provided relatively few medium- and long-term
credits for small- to medium-sized agro-processing and marketing projects. Based
on the results of the Second Agricultural Development Project (Ln. 2561-EGT), the
project envisages a growing demand for medium-term lending for agro-processing
activities in rural areas.   The project would provide investment financing
through the banking system, as well as technical support through the FNRI and the
regional extension and research stations; in addition, the project would help
promote dissemination of market and price information.
3.09        Institutional strengthening of the technology transfer agencies as
well as the banking sector is critical to the success of the project. Although,
commercial banks are still hesitant about participating in the project now, the
project design contains provision for training of their staff in rural banking
and for sharing of information on rural investment opportunities, so as to induce
them to participate in the project in due course. In the meanwhile, the PBDAC
will remain the credit agency for the rural sector and will act as the *apex"
agency to on-lend the proceeds of the proposed loan and credit. Consequently,
besides improving the resource base and streamlining of PBDAC to enhance its
rural banking capablities, the project would foster interaction among the
various agents involved, i.e. the producers, bankers, extensionists, technology
transfer specialists and SMSs, researchers, traders and marketing agents.
Training of relevant staff and effective monitoring and evaluation of performance
would be carried out under the project. The project would also support a system
of incentives for good performance by staff engaged in credit and extension
activities.
D. Project Objectives
3.10        The project's key objectives are tot (i) increase agricultural
productivity by promoting appropriate technological packages at the farm level;
(i$) increase rural income by creating off-farm job opportunities in storage,
processing and marketing activities, trading in agricultural inputs, contractual
and custom hire services, and agro-based enterprises; (iii) streamline PBOAC's
organization and strengthen staff capabilities to enhance its performance as an
effective rural financial institution; (iv) facilitate participation of
commercial banks in supporting rural investments, and (v) improve capabilities
of relevant institutions to bring about effectlve coordination among the various
agencies engaged in the provision of credit, technology transfer, and marketing
services.
B. Project DesoriRtion
3.11        The project would be national in scope and the line of credit
component in the project would support investments in modernizing farming
practices and post-harvest handling of produce in both the old Lands and the
reclaimed New Lands. It would finances
(a)   on-farm technoloav improvement (US$115.0 million) which would
cover selective mechanization of land levelling, seed-bed



- 25 -
preparation, on-farm irrigation, seeding, crop husbandry,
agro-chemicals and pesticides application, and harvesting;
(b)   aoro-based enterorises (US$95.0 million) for establishing:
custom hire services for farming operations, warehousing,
processing and marketing of produce; and
(c)   institutional development (US$59.0 million) of: (i) the PLDAC
and other participating banks to strengthen their rural
banking activities, such as deposit mobilization and rural
investn.ents, PBDAC to divest its non-banking activities and
streamline its organization, construct and establish 20
village banks, and train relevant staff in banking skills; and
(ii) the relevant agencies of the MALR to disseminate improved
production and processing technology and promote sound farm
management practices, provide market information to producers,
and facilitate better coordination between credit, research,
extension, processing and marketing.
P. Detailed Project Features
3.12        The project consists of a line of credit to provide funds to
agricultural and agro-processing machinery dealers and manufacturers, input and
output traders, farmers, and investors to meet their investment and incremental
working capital requirements. Details of the mechanism for utilizing the line
of credit are contained in para. 4.12. While the line of credit will not be
earmarked for specific uses, the criteria for its use would emphasize utilization
of improved farm technology packages and investments in agro-processing and
marketing services.
3.13        On-farm Technological Imvrovement.  Major issues to be addressed
through this component are: efficient use of water and inputs; modernization of
farm practices to improve timeliness and cost effectiveness, and enhancement of
productivity of reclaimed lands through introduction of appropriate technology
for various categories of farmers. A well-coordinated mechanization program
would: (a) help the sector overcome seasonal farm labor shortages; (b) increase
yields through the introduction of improved seeding and crop husbandry practices;
(c) reduce intervals between crops and enable more intensive use of land by
following optimal planting and harvest dates; (d) increase labor productivity;
and (e) create new investment and job opportunities, particularly in local
manufacturing and operation and maintenance of agricultural equipment, and in
provision of agricultural mechanization services to farmers.
3.14        The core element of the technology package is the adoption of
mechanized row planting and inter-row crop maintenance practices.   Use of
improved land levelling, pressurized irrigation, seed drilling, fertilizer
drilling, and integrated pest management are components of the package.  To
modernize agricultural practices and to intensify land use, tractor units have
to be versatile, with power levels and capacity to handle multiple tasks in a
single pass. Such tractor units are expensive. Given the small-size holdings
which characterize the Egyptian farming sector, provision of custom hire services
of equipment is key to modernizing farming activities. As noted before, farmers
already utilize custom hire services offered by other farmers, entrepreneurs or
cooperatives to supplement their own machinery capacity. Since Egyptian farmers
raise various crops, the project will promote utilization of versatile equipment



- 26 -
and upgrade the quality of field operations by introducing improved machinery
such as better designed chisel plows.  It will provide a range of machinery
suited for specific areas and crops, such as disc harrows for light soils, rotary
tillers for seed-bed preparation in heavy soils, and small tractors for
horticultural areas. Trials carried out in Egypt which combined row seeding and
improved irrigation has shown that yields of field crops increase by some 20 to
30 percent, with water savings of the same magnitude. Use of precision planters
and seed drills for row seeding has resulted in some 10 percent reCuction in seed
and fertilizer use, improved crop stands, and yield increases of 10 to 15
percent.
3.15        In order to ensure adequate availability of machinery and spare
parts, the project would provide financing to the banking system through a line
of credit administered by the PBDAC as the *apex" agency. In this context, it
should be noted that, although a free foreign exchange market exists in Egypt and
Egyptian banks have sufficient access to these resources, the agricultural sector
has been unable to tap this market as the banks have not given priority attention
to service the needs of the sector. This is largely because of the lack of
adequate institutional arrangements for field-level demonstration of new
technology, as well as lack of familiarity of the banking system with rural
investment opportunities.   By placing particular emphasis on field-level
demonstrations through Regional Research and Extension Stations (RRESs) and
Extension Information and Demonstration Units (RIDUs), the project seeks to
remedy the weakness in technology dissemination; similarly, by training of bank
staff in rural credit and by sharing information on rural investment
opportunities, the project seeks to mobilize commercial banks' participation in
the project.   The proceeds of the loan would be channelled through PBDAC's
branches and the 8DAC network as well as qualified participating banks.
Financing would be made available for import of agricultural machinery and
equipment, components, spare parts, and goods by dealers and manufacturers, as
well as custom hire operators, cooperatives and farmers. It is expected that
some 480,000 feddans would benefit from row planting and inter-row cultivation.
Taking into account increased farmer interest in the production of horticultural
crops, the project would finance, inter alia, equipment and supplies for green
houses, as well as mid-sized tractors for use in orchard cultivation. It would
also finance on-farm irrigation improvements. To meet the needs of the reclaimed
New Lands, where soils have a lower water holding capacity, and to promote more
efficient irrigation in appropriate cases in the Old Lands, the project would
finance investments in high pressure pumping units, pressurized sprinklers, drip
irrigation systems, and equipment for fertigation.
3.16        In  order  to   finance  improved  technology  packages,  some
rationalization of the present organization of PBDAC would be necessary.
Functions relating to on-farm investment financing within PBDAC are now
distributed among different organization units, such as production requirements,
machines, agricultural mechanization, development requirements, storage and
supply, development projects, rural development, etc.   It is necessary to
consolidate these activities into a single department of PBDAC and provide the
department with needed equipment and technical know-how.
3.17        Dissemination of newly generated technology as well as available
technology would become the responsibility of the RRESs. Of the 10 RRESs, three
-- one each in the Delta, Upper Egypt, and New Lands -- have been selected for
upgrading, on a pilot basis, under the project. The activities of these stations
would draw upon skills available not only in the ARC and the extension service,



- 27 -
but also in universities, the private sector, and the farming community. Field
demonstrations would be an important means of disseminating technology.
3.18        Agro-based Enterprises. There is no earmarking of funds for specific
sub-sectors under the project. The investments envisaged would, inte* alia,
cover: (a) modernization or rehabilitation of existing lines, such as wheat and
rice mills, edible oil extraction units, cheese processing, drying of fruits and
vegetables, etc.; (b) dealerships and/or workshops supplying and servicing post-
harvest activities, dairying and veterinary services, handling, storage,
transport, conditioning and processing equipment; (c) new processing lines and
equipment, such as, tomato juice extraction and production of tomato-based
bouillon cubes, etc.; and (d) other small agro-based enterprises engaged in
distribution.   Beneficiaries are expected to provide about 30 per cent of
investment cost and permanent working capital requirement; term credit would
provide the remaining 70 per cent. Financing would be provided for machinery,
equipment, spare parts, information services, software, office technology, and
buildings. Financing of storage, transport and marketing facilities, which are
badly needed in the New Lands, would receive particular attention under the
project.
3.19        With the anticipated expansion of demand for agro-based operations,
the existing units within PBDAC which deal with agro-industry would be
consolidated into an "agro-based enterprises department." The department would
be headed by a Director, assisted by three technical staff with qualifications
and experience in marketing and agro-processing; in addition, an information
center would be set up, inter alia, to assemble and maintain technical data and
operate a resource library. Technical assistance of 24 person-months would be
provided under the project for upgrading staff capabilities and improve services
needed for expanded lending. The project will finance small- to medium-scale
(i.e., with assets not exceeding US$3.0 million) agro-based enterprises. The
department would establish a register of competent technology and business
consultants who are qualified to assist potential investors prepare bankable
project proposals and feasibility studies for farm-related business and agro-
industrial investment, prepare marketing studies, or provide market intelligence
and business advice. This would be a service provided by the PBDAC for promoting
diversification of the rural economy; access to the consultant's register will
not be restricted. While the initial costs of this service would be borne by
PEDAC, it could consider levying a reasonable charge to investors utilizing the
service.
3.20        PBDAC  would  conduct  appropriate  market  and  sector  analyses
periodically to determine investment demand in these areas and share the
information with participating banks and the investment community.  Possible
areas of study include the scope for investments in custom hire services and farm
input trading, post-harvest handling, conditioning (including milling), packing,
storing, transporting, processing and marketing of cereals and oil seeds, fruits
and vegetables, animal products, etc.
3.21        Institutional DeveloDment.  Modernization of agriculture requires
coordination of services and resources from both the public and private sector.
Experience demonstrates that a diffused approach of dealing with several agencies
would be counterproductive, and that designating a single agency to mobilize the
necessary services is more cost-effective and efficient. The Project Management
Department (PMD) located in PBDAC would provide this focal point.



- 28 -
3.22        Technical assistance totaling 96 person-months of international
experts and 335 person-months of local experts has been provided for under the
project to: (a) assist PBDAC to upgrade its planning, accounting, banking and
loan recovery, and new types of lending capabilities (including agro-industrial
lending) and establish environmental appraisal and management capacity; and (b)
assist the regional and sub-regional research, extension information and
demonstration centers to mobilize expertise from various sources for technology
development, demonstration and dissemination in both production and processing
activities.
3.23        Training would be key to achieving the objectives of the project.
The project would expand and improve the training program of PBDAC and
participating commercial banks.   It would emphasize training of branch and
village bank officers and staff. Local training in such key areas as appraisal,
monitoring, supervision and recovery of loans, loan classification and
provisioning, rehabilitation of problem accounts and accounting and MIS
reporting, would receive attention.  In the case of PBDAC and BDAC staff in
particular, attention would be devoted to analysis of agricultural and agro-
processing projects, sector risks, project implementation and loan monitoring,
review and analysis of business operations especially deposit mobilization and
financial management, and incorporating environmental considerations within loan
appraisal and monitoring procedures. Overseas training for about 390 staff on
short visits of two to twelve weeks amounting to 770 person-months has been
included in the project (para. 3.26). Main fields of training ares project
appraisal, market study, deposit mobilization, private sector dealers' support,
small enterprises, new credit lines, loan collection, banking services, rural
enterprises and village program, internal audit, and women in development. In
addition, PBDAC would enter into a twinning arrangement with a similar, but more
mature, agricultural credit institution in another country. Selection of the
twin agency (or agencies) would be made in consultation with the Bank by December
31, 1994.
3.24        The project also provides for a study on rural financial markets;
this study would b--: completed by December 31, 1994 (para. 4.21). Its results
will be used to improve rural banking services and increase rural savings
mobilization. PBDAC would share with the commercial banks the findings of this
study.
3.25        Rationalization of PBDAC's staffing as well as retraining and
redeployment of personnel needed for its banking operations would be supported
under the project (paras. 2.26 and 3.24). The early retirement program is a
voluntary one and the "bonus" for early retirement targets mainly mid-grade level
staff (i.e. unit chiefs, researchers and clerical staff) to avail of the offer.
The average amount of compensation for such early retirees is estimated at LB
15,434 (or US$4,635) each. Project training funds would be used to cover the
local training costs of some 2,800 staff presently engaged in trading activities
and facilitate their absorption into banking operations.   As part of the
restructuring activities, PBDAC would prepare an assessment of its staff-mix
requirements and establish targets for retirement or retraining and redeployment
of excess staff.  Assurances were obtained at neaotiations that PBDAC would
Provide the Bank by September 30. 1994. with its staff retirement and trainino
Proaram nroDosal 4Daras. 4.19 and 5.03(e)}.
3.26        Village banks are in dire need of upgrading to attract customers and
the PBDAC's management is already engaged in rehabilitating or reconstructing
village banks which are in a run-down condition. In addition, it is necessary



- 29 -
to open bank offices in the New Lands. Taking these factors into account, the
project provides for 20 village banks to be constructed (or rehabilitated) and
equipped. Assurances were obtained at neaotiations that PBDAC would provide the
Bank by September 30, 1994. the justification for orogoging new offices.
arrangements for site accuisition. and estimated costs and eoeScted benefits from
the o2enina of these branches ioara. S.03(fil.  There will be two types of
building designs, one for a large village bank and another for a small village
bank and these designs have been found acceptable.
3.27        The technoloav transfer component includes promotion of (a) on-far
production technologies, and (b) rural enterprise development. The project,
while operating within the existing institutional framework, will add some
critical skills needed and establish the linkages required to bring about
effective use of available technology. To this end, the project will test the
appropriateness of the proposed system by upgrading three RRESs - at Sakha in the
Delta, Sids in Upper Egypt, and Nubariya in the reclaimed area - designated by
the Government. The design of the pilot program takes into account the need for
decentralized operations according to the agro-climatic zone.   It seeks to
establish close collaboration between the various agents involved - i.e.
research, extension, credit, input suppliers and farmers. It emphasizes cost
effectiveness and, if successfully implemented, it would, in the long term,
enhance the effectiveness of agricultural services while reducing research and
extension outlays. The RRES in Sids will provide office space to the staff, but
this would need to be added in Sakha and Nubariya. The project will provide
furniture, various equipment, materials, and vehicles. it will also provide for
a limited amount of laboratory and agro-industrial prototype equipment for the
FNRt. At Nubariya station in New Lands, additional accommodation as well as
infrastructural facilities will be built in order to strengthen research and on-
farm activities, and to establish an irrigation demonstration farm. Pour SMSs,
one each in farm mechanization, on-farm water use, production economics and
marketing, and agro-processing, will be assigned to each of the three REESe.
These SMSs will be drawn from the concerned institutes of the ARC. They will
work  full  time  in the project.    The  staff will  be  selected based on
qualifications, relevant experience, and commitment to the program. Assurances
were obtained at negotiations that 12 SMSs would be selected and assioned to the
three RRESs by October 31, 1994 fpara. 5.03(all. The training to be provided
under the project envisages 30 person-months of overseas training and 63 person
months of local training (including 21 person-months of environmental training)
for selected RRES staff.
3.28        The link between the RRESs and the extension service would be
strengthened through 33 Extension Information and Demonstration Units (EIDUS)
belonging to the Extension Service.  Each EIDU is designed to serve 10,000
farmers. Each EIDU will have one extensionist, who will be the head of the unit,
and four technology transfer specialists (TTS), one each in farm mechanization,
on-farm water management, production economic and marketing, and agro-processing.
All the staff will be drawn from the existing cadre of extension services in the
governorates, and they will be carefully selected. Assurances were obtained at
neaotiations that to each of the 13 selected RIDUs. an experienced extensionist
and four technoloav transfer specialists would be assioned by October 31. 1994
loara. 5.03(gc).  The project would provide adequate office accommodation and
physical facilities for farmers, visits, meetings, exhibitions, audio-visual
programs and on-farm demonstrations. The EIDU in Nubariya will, in addition,
need residential accommodation as no private or government accommodation is
available at the sites. The existing VENs in areas served by the BIDUs will also
participate in the program.  The project provides for 20 person-months of



- 30 -
overseas training and 972 person-months of local training for staff drawn from
the extension service.  The major target for local train-.ng would be the TTSs.
VEWs will generally receive on-the-job training while working with TTss.
Training of farmers would be done mainly through demonstrations and meetings.
These activities would be decentralized and demand driven and would be undertaken
after assessment of response of farmers to new technological options. Training
will also be provided to custom hire operators, as well as farmers and agro-
industrial investors through appropriate training centers.
3.29        Provision of Credit and Extension Support to Women.  The PBDAC has,
in recent years, begun giving special attention to provision of credit to women
farmers and investors. It has 10 staff trained in promoting lending to women and
there are 161 village banks with lending programs for women. It is proposed to
target 350 village banks to extend loans to women during 1994. Arrangements
exist already for lending to womens' groups and to accept group guarantees for
such loans. The average borrowing by women tends to be relatively small, at LE
900, and loans are utilized for farming, agro-processing and other productive
activities. During FY93, there were 21,000 women who borrowed from the PBDAC.
With regard to extension services for women farmers, there are at present 780
female staff, including 242 female extensionists. MALR envisages a more active
role for women in extension services and training programs are in place to
achieve this objective. The project includes specific training courses to staff
in credit and extension services to women.
G.  Proiect Cost and Financina Plan
(a) Cost Estimates
3.30        The project costs (including contingencies) are estimated at LE 1,081
million (US$268.8 million) of which the foreign exchange cost is LE 460.7 million
(US$113.6 million) or 42 per cent of total cost. The machinery, equipment and
vehicle prices are based on the prevailing retail prices in Egypt in June 1993.
Consultant services have been estimated at a unit cost of US$15,000 per month for
foreign experts and LE 2,000 per month for local experts. Import duties and
sales taxes are reflected in the calculation of the project cost.  Physical
contingencies have been included for civil works at a rate of 10 per cent. Price
contingencies in US Dollar terms amount to 10 per cent of the base cost, on the
basis of annual international and local inflation rates as follows:

for modernisation (e.g. laser levelling, pressurized irrigation equipment, small
multi-crop combines, small reapers and binders, etc.) accounts for US$79.0

cent of total project cost). The total cost of institutional development is
estimated US$58.7 million (or 22 per cent of total project cost). A substantial
portion of this (US$42.0 million) would be directed towards technical assistance
and training to upgrade rural banking capabilities of the PBDAC and participating
banks, rehabilitation and expansion of some 20 rural banks, and payments for
early retirees and for training for redeployment of excess staff in PBDAC. The
cost of strengthening RRESs and EIDUs is estimated at US$17.0 million.

3.34        The project will facilitate access to the Egyptian agricultural
machinery and equipment market for suppliers from all Bank member countries and
Taiwan, China. There are no import bans which affect the import needs of the
project: and, significant progress has been achieved in reducing tariff levels
under the ongoing ERSAP which ia being closely monitored by the Bank. Import
tariffs for tractors (20 to 85 hp) have been reduced to 20 per cent, and for most
other items, the import tariffs are 5 per cent.) Procedures for import and for
issue of letters of credit have been simplified. Specifically, the Government
has rescinded the requirement of prior approval of the Ministry of Military
Production to permit import of agricultural equipment which the military
factories are licensed to produce. The competitive environment which prevails
in the agricultural machinery market ensures unhindered access to the market by
suppliers and availability of modern technology to farmers and investors. The
ongoing Second Agricultural Development Project (Ln. 2561-EGT) has already
enabled the establishment of 83 local dealerships for machinery manufacturers.
A wide range of foreign manufacturers of agricultural machinery and equipment are
already represented in Egypt. The project would potentially increase the number
of suppliers further and promote strong competition and increased range of
choices of equipment for custom hire operators and other beneficiaries. The PMD
would ensure that the procurement methods established under the Loan and Credit
are followed. The procurement arrangements agreed at loan negotiations ensure
sufficient notice to be given internationally, so that manufacturers from all
Bank member countries would have an equal opportunity to establish agencies in
Egypt and qualify for financing under the project.
3.35        Procurement of eaui=ment would respond to market demand involving
numerous dealerships and beneficiaries and a wide range of equipment to be
imported by the private sector periodically throughout the   implementation
period. It is not feasible to "package" the number of contracts. Most import
orders would be in the range of USS5,000 to US$300,000. No contract is expected
to be valued at more than USS500,000. The import orders would be too dispersed
and small-scale to be suitable for international competitive bidding (ICB).
Availability of after sales service is of critical importance in the choice of
machinery and equipment by the beneficiaries. Procurement of vehicles and office
eauiument and laboratory eauinment for the PMD, PBDAC, RRESs and EIDUs are
subject to ICB in accordance with Bank Guidelines "Procurement under IBRD Loans
and IDA Credits (May 1992).0 Egyptian manufacturers competing under ICB would
receive a preference in bid evaluation of 15 per cent of the CIF price, or the
prevailing custom duty applicable to non-exempt importers, whichever is less,
provided they can prove that the manufacturing cost of such goods includes a
value added in Egypt equal to at least 20 per cent of the ex-factory price of
such goods. Imports by dealers of aaricultural inwuts, such as improved seeds
and planting materials, compounded fertilizers, veterinary drugs, animal feed,
etc., tend not to exceed US$100,000 individually in most instances and would be
procured under established procedures that work efficiently; in rare instances
where such procurement exceeds US$1.0 million, it would be subject to ICB
procedures.   Contracts for the provision of technical assistance would be in
accordance with Bank Guidelines for thi Use of Consultants (August 1981), and
subject to Bank approval.   Consultant contracts of more than US$100,000
equivalent with consulting firms and more than US$50,000 equivalent for
individuals will be subject to prior review by IDA.
3.36        Civil works expenditures for rehabilitation,  expansion or new
construction of buildings for village banks and RRESs and EIDU, would be subject
to local competitive bidding, since these works would be relatively small (each
building is estimated to cost less than US$50,000) and are in dispersed



- 35 -
locations, and therefore not suitable for ICB procurement.   Local bidding
procedures for these small contracts which have been applied in other Bank-
financed projects carried out by the PBDAC, are acceptable to the Bank.
3.37        The PMD would sample about 5 per cent of contracts under US$Soo,000
equivalent, and review at least ten contracts in the first year of the project
to ensure compliance with the covenants. The documentation would be kept in the
PHD for selective review by Bank supervision missions {para. 5.03(h)).
1. Disbursement
3.38        The proposed Bank loan US$54.0 million and the portion of the IDA
credit which is earmarked for on-lending purposes (US$46.0 million equivalent)
cover all foreign expenditures of machinery, equipment, and supply of goods, and
the remaining portion of the IDA credit (US$21.0 million equivalent) would be
applied to the institutional development component of the project. Disbursement
would be made in accordance with Guidelines for Withdrawal of Proceeds of IBRD
Loans and IDA Credits (November 1985). Retroactive financing of up to US$1.0
million equivalent (about 1.5 per cent of the Credit) has been provided under the
IDA Credit to cover eligible expenditures incurred after February 1, 1994. The
retroactive financing proposed will facilitate timely launching of the project.
The loan and credit proceeds are expected to be fully disbursed over a period of
about eight years which is the standard disbursement profile for Bank-financed
projects in Egypt.
3.39        To facilitate project execution, PBDAC would establish: (i) a Special
Account in a commercial bank, in US dollars, with an initial deposit of US$2.0
million out of the Bank loan; and (ii) another Speclal Account in a commercial
bank, in US dollars, with an initial deposit of US$2.0 million out of the IDA
credit. Disbursement would be made against Statements of Expenditures (SOE) or
directly against letters of credit. Except for consultants' contracts of over
US$20,000, contracts valued at less than US$50,000 equivalent would be financed
under sOE procedures. Relevant documentation in support of SOEs would not be
submitted to the Bank, but would be retained by the PMD and made available to
Bank supervision missions and to ir.dependent auditors.   Applications for
replenishment of the Special Account would be submitted monthly, or whenever the
Special Account is drawn to 50 per cent of the initial deposit, whichever occurs
first.

categories is shown in Table 3.4.

The project completion is expected by June 30, 2001, and it i8 anticipated that
the Bank loan and IDA credit will be fully disbursed by that date. The Closing
Date would, therefore, be June 30, 2001. The estimated schedule of disbursement

IV. PtROJNY IXPLMONTATION
A. PBDAC As Proiect Implementing Institution
4.01        Project implementation would be the responsibility of the PBDAC which
would has established a new Project Management Department (PMD) for this purpose.
The Bank loan of US$54.0 million would be made to the PBDAC with the guarantee
of the Government of Egypt. The IDA credit of SDR 48.7 million would be made to
the Government for on-lending to the PBDAC, out of which SDR 33.4 million
(USS46.0 million equivalent) would be utilized for sub-loans to investors, and
SDR 15.3 million (US$21.0 million equivalent) would be used for institutional
development. The Government of the Arab Republic of Egypt would provide the loan
guarantee for a fee of 0.125 per cent. PBDAC would bear the foreign exchange
risk; to cover its risk, PBDAC would pay a fee to the Ministry of Finance. This
fee ts currently determined to be three per cent of each maturity. The Ministry
of Finance will review this rate at mid-term and adjust it periodically
thereafter during the maturity period of the loan, so as to reflect market
considerations, i.e, to make the loan to be in line with the changes in the value
of the Egyptian pound.
4.02        PBDAC has been designated as the most appropriate apex institution
to implement the project. The choice of PBDAC to play this role is based on the
following considerations: (a) It has demonstrated its capabilities in utilizing
credit as an effective vehicle for disseminating new technologies with economic
potential to the farming community and rural entrepreneurs. In so doing, it has
established effective linkages with extension staff utilizing a performance-based
system of incentives for those engaged in promoting modern farming practices. (b)
PBDAC has also established effective links with agricultural machinery dealers
under the ongoing Second Agricultural Development Project (Ln. 2561-EGT) and is
familiar with procurement and disbursement procedures under Bank loans. (c) In
line with the liberalization program, PBDAC has substantially divested its
trading and distribution activities and has trained and financed private
investors to perform these activlties. (d) Credit availability is a key factor
in adoption of modern farming practices and development of agro-based
enterprises, and PBDAC, with its network of village banks, is well positioned to
make credit available to the rural community. Besides, it will collaborate
closely with other commercial banks which might be qualified to participate in
the project. Since modernization involves collaboration among various agents
involved in the process, effective coordination would be important. The PHD
located in PBDAC would be the focal point for project implementation.
4.03        The prospect of effective participation by commercial banks in the
project hinges on: (a) the cost of loan funds and the margin of profitability it
affords to PBs, (b) staff skills needed for rural operations, and (c) willingness
of commercial banks to comply with Dank's loan conditionality and supervision
procedures. However, it is anticipated that as the project gets started, a
select number of commercial banks may develop interest in the project; therefore,
the project has been designed to facilitate their participation. By providing
access to project funded technical assistance and training, there is scope for
commercial banks to familiarize themselves with rural banking and investment and
to have an adequate cadre of staff trained in such operations.
4.04        The eligibility of commercial banks to participate in the project
would be subject to Bank approval and would be based on their full compliance
with the prudential regulations of the CBE; the CBE has agreed to confirm



- 38 -
compliance in this regard. In addition, PBs will need to have their financial
accounts audited by private external auditors, in accordance with internationally
accepted auditing practices, and submit a copy of the audit report to the Bank.
4.05        Soon after the loan and credit are approved by the Bank and IDA,
PBDAC would write to the major commercial banks informing them of the project
details and loan and credit arrangements, as well as the terms and conditions
which would apply with regard to the use of the funds.   Subsidiary Loan
Agreements would be entered into between PBDAC and each commercial bank qualified
to be a PB. The terms of proposed onlending would be satisfactory to the Bonk.
Assurances in this regard were obtained at loan neaotiations {para. 5.03(i)3.
The Subsidiary Loan Agreements should be executed not later than 12 months after
the loan and credit become effective. A supervision mission at the end of the
first year of the project would examine the interest and extent of participation
of commercial banks and make amendments to project arrangements as appropriate.
B.  Project Imnlementation Arrangements
4.06        The Chairman of PBDAC has been designated as the Project Director
responsible for project implementation.   He would head a Project Steering
Committee (PSC) which would guide and monitor project implementation. Membership
of the PSC would include the Environmental Advisor of PBDAC, high-level
Government representatives from the MALR, Ministries of Finance, and
International Cooperation, and the Agricultural Research Center (ARC), as well
as private sector representatives from the banking, machinery and input trading,
and farming communities. The PSC would be responsible for guiding and monitoring
project implementation. It would meet at least semi-annually; more frequent
meetings would be appropriate in the initial stages of project implementation.
A full-time Project Coordinator and two Deputy Directors would assist the Project
Director in the implementation of the project. one of the Deputy Directors would
deal with project components involving technology transfer, while the other would
deal with banking-related issues involving the PBDAC, BDACs and other PBs. The
Project Coordinator would act as the Secretary of the PSC.   The Project
Management Department (PMD) would be managed on a day-to-day basis by the Project
Coordinator. The PHD would retain adequate numbers of full-time staff who are
familiar with project administration and Bank procedures of disbursement,
procurement and progress reporting. Necessary incentives and training would be
provided to the staff of the PMD as well as those in the field with specific
responsibilities for promoting new technologies and types of investment
activities.
4.07        In order to promote the project and streamline relevant procedures
to effectively meet the expectations of the beneficiaries of the project, an
Implementation Advisory Committee (IAC) has been established. The IAC includes
representatives of agricultural machinery and equipment dealers, agro-industrial
investors, and farmersl besides, members representing the MALR, concerned ARC
institutes and universities and the Egyptian Environmental Affairs Agency (EEAA)
have been appointed to the committee. The Project Coordinator will act as the
Secretary of the IAC as well. Meetings of the IAC will be held at least semi-
annually, and the outcome of the meetings will be reported to the PSC.
4.08        Status  of  Implementation  Arranoements.    In  accordance  with
understandings reached at project appraisal, the Borrower has already established
the PHD and designated the Project Director, the Project Coordinator and two
Deputy Directors.  In addition, the PSC and IAC have been established with



- 39 -
representation as outlined in para. 4.07 above. An office of Environmental
Adviser has also been established in the PBDAC. These actions demonstrate the
Borrower's commitment to launch the project promptly (paras. 4.06 and 4.07).
4.09        Dissemination of Technoloav.  XALR will be responsible to carry out
this component with the support of concerned Deputy Director in the PMD, who will
act in close liaison with the Director General of ARC and the First
Undersecretary for Agricultural Extension.   Each MRES will designate a team
leader from among the four SMSs (one each in on-farm water management,
mechanization, agro-based industry, and economics and marketing) to coordinate
the activities of the four SMSs involved in the agricultural modernization
components specified in the project and provide the work program and budget
needs.    Based primarily on proposals received from the RRESs, the PMD will
allocate  funds.   The Deputy Director of the PMD will convene   periodic
consultative meetings with a small group representing the ARC Institutes
concerned (the AERI, FNRI, SWRI and AERI) and the team leaders of the three pilot
RRESs to review progress  in project implementation.   Since exchange of
information and experience will be vital for the spread of technology, the RRES
team leaders will organize semi-annually joint workshops with representatives of
the PBDAC/BDAC, directors of extension of the concerned governorates, members of
the agricultural faculty in the local university, private input and commodity
traeers, machinery dealers, and selected members of agro-industrial investors and
farmers. These regional workshop meetings will review achievements and future
work plans and render assistance in facilitating coordination among the various
agents of change in the local community.   The Director of Extension of the
Governorate concerned will designate competent extension officials to manage each
of the EIDUs and the field program. The designated extensionist who will be the
leader of the group would, in collaboration with the TTSs, prepare and handle
audio-visual materials. The TTSs, with the support of SMSs, will plan and carry
out demonstration programs. The TTSs will interact closely with the farmers,
VEWs, credit officers, machinery and input suppliers, and custom hire operators
in order to secure their participation in the demonstration programs.
4.10        The key element of program planning would be that it should be demand
driven and decentralized to the level of the beneficiaries, i.e. the farming
communities. The TTSs in each EIDU will identify specific problems and needs of
farmers and, in collaboration with the SMSs, formulate a three-year rolling
program. The program will be indicative in nature and adapted as circumstances
warrant. Annual work programs will be extracted from the three-year program.
The Deputy Director of PMD in-charge of technology components will compile the
program and submit it to PSC for approval not later than March 31 of each year.
The Project Director will assume responsibility for program approval. Approved
programs for the three pilot regions will be compiled together and distributed
to all EIDUs as a means of sharing knowledge.
4.11        Since EIDUs will be the hub for transfer of technology, there would
be special attention paid to staff training.   At the start of the project,
designated SMSs will  receive training  in the relevant ARC  institute to
familiarize themselves with relevant technology options and visit machinery
manufacturers and university stations as necessary. Provision has been made for
22 person-months of short-term overseas training for selected SMSs and ARC staff
involved in the project; similarly, 20 person-months of overseas training is
provided for TTSs and extension staff. Provision has also been made for in-
country training of the various agents involved in the project, such as farmers,
custom hire operators, and extensionists. At the beginning of each season, a
one-week workshop of SMSs and TTS5 will be held in each RRES to define plans for



- 40 -
implementation of the program for the season. Besides on-farm demonstrations,
the EIDUs will hold farmers' meetings and exhibitions to present new technology.
The meetings will be used to disseminate not only knowledge of technological
options, their costs, benefits, merits and demerits, but also market information,
environmental messages, safety measures in handling machinery and chemical
inputs, etc. The program will be gradually developed by each EIDU to cover a
target population of 10,000 farmers during the project implementation period.
The success of the program will be assessed based not merely on the number of
demonstrations conducted or farmers contacted, but more importantly on demand
generated for new equipment or practices, as well as loans requested.
4.12        On-Lending Arranaements.  PBDAC would utilize the proceeds of the
loan for onlending through the BDACs, as well as PBs. The onlending interest
rate would be sufficient to cover Bank's variable interest rate, plus the
commitment charges, guarantee fee, the foreign exchange risk premium, and a small
administrative overhead (of 0.5 per cent) to compensate PBDAC for its costs in
being the "apex" agency.  onlending of the loan proceeds to BDACs and PBs would
be on identical terms. Foreign exchange needed by importers of agricultural and
agro-industrial equipment, components, spare parts and inputs would be made
available by the PBDAC, BDACs and PBs either through a "cash window" or a "credit
window".  In the case of the "cash window", foreign exchange is provided to
clients against an equivalent amount of local currency payment, and the local
currency proceeds of these transactions would be utilized for financing rural
credit needs. These arrangements have worked satisfactorily under the Second
Agricultural Development Project (Ln. 2561-EGT). Alternatively, clients would
be offered term credit (i.e. "credit window").  Such term loans, which would be
appropriate for borrowers of sizable amounts for investment in equipment, agro-
processing enterprises, etc., would be subject, inter ala, to appraisal
procedures to evaluate economic, financial and environmental viability of
proposed investments. Interest rates for credit window customers would be market
determined. In instances where the sub-loan exceeds US$1.0 million equivalent,
the appraisal should be submitted to the Bank for prior review and comments.
4.13        PBDAC's credit manual sets out sub-loan purposes, sub-loan size,
interest, sub-borrowers' contribution, collateral, appraisal and approval
procedures, and repayment periods. There is a committee which oversees the
manual and approves changes as required. While these procedures and terms and
conditions of lending are deemed satisfactory, there is need to modify the
manual to provide for environmental assessmerts for selected operations. PBDAC's
Business Plan recognizes the need to incorporate environmental considerations
within the loan appraisal and monitoring procedures. Assurances were obtained
at necotiations that environmental screenina auidelines. consistent with the
Bank's Operational Directive N_o.OD 4.01 and national criteria for environmental
assessment. would be oreoared for use by the staff of PBDAC. BDACo and PBs
{paras. 4.33 and 5.03(k)}.
4.14        Approval of medium- and long-term lending for sub-projects would
continue to be supported by adequate technical and financial appraisals. For
relatively new areas of activity, i.e. loans for marketing and processing, the
content and methods of appraisal would be suitably expanded and refined to
include environmental assessment. Provision has been made in project financing
to enable employment of necessary experts on a short-term basis to assist the
PBDAC in the review, appraisal and supervision of these investments.
4.15        PBDAC's loan disbursement and supervision arrangements ensure that
expenditures are in conformity with the applicable lending agreements. Desk



- 41 -
checking is supplemented by field visits to sub-projects. Follow-up reports on
each visit would comment on utilization of sub-loan funds, compare actual with
projected operations, review sub-borrower's management and financial competence
and make recommendations. This report should, in future, include an assessment
of environmental impact and propose mitigation plans where necessary.
C. Loan Recovery
4.16        PBDAC would define loan classification and set up provisioning as
required by the CBE. The required provisions for possible loan losses would
better reflect the quality of the portfolios as charges for non-performing loans
would be immediately reflected in each branch/village bank's profit and loss
account.    Staff training would be strengthened to promote bank-customer
relationship and carry out proper loan appraisal and supervision. PBs will have
access to such training.   Although PBDAC's loan recovery record has remained
remarkably sound in the past, it would face increasingly higher risks in new
lending areas with the divestiture of its trading activities; therefore, possible
future loan losses are likely to be greater. To protect itself against such a
situation, PBDAC will raise its provisions for loan losses in stages, rising from
ten per cent in FY 1993 to 12 per cent in FY 1997. Required increases in loan
provisions will necessitate modest infusion of capit& by the Government. This
infusion will be of practical value only if overdues of new loans can be reduced.
Several measures, including policies on loan classification, provisioning and
suspension of interest accruals, improved loan appraisal and supervision and
effective staff training have been proposed to improve loan recovery.
D.  Strenathening PBDAC's Organization
4.17        To consolidate its organization ar  current activi.ties, PBDAC has
been filling key management vacancies.   Furtiier changes being considered by
PBDAC's management comprise of reorganization and restructuring of PBDAC and
affiliated EDACs, qualitative improvement of staff skills through training and
provision of technical assistance, enhancing financial policies, improved
performance monitoring and reporting, deposit mobilization, and further
diversification of the bank's portfolio. Various actions have been initiated
towards this end, as summarized earlier. PBDAC has also prepared a comprehensive
medium-term Business Plan together with an Operational Action Plan (Annex 2, Part
B) which incorporates these elements. The plan, which will be rolling plan, will
be updated annually; and annual targets to be reached will be established in the
p an {para. 5.03(d)).
4.18        PBDAC, with the support of the USAID, is carrying out an organization
and management study to review its structure with a view to improving the
profitability of its banking business, covering, inter liIa, reorganization of
branches and village banks to eliminate lose centers, control of expenses,
revision of the branch network after careful assessment of profitability and
expansion of profitable banking services. It would also include organizational
and financial analysis of rural banking operations and administration. Among the
major activities would be an analysis of administrative costs and personnel costs
at each level of the organization.   This data would be used to assess the
financial viability of business units throughout the PBDAC system.
4.19        The study would also assess and propose actions to strengthen
financial management which could be improved and operationalized at every level
of the organization. Performance ratios would be calculated at each level and



- 42 -
would be made part of the financial statements of each business unit, treated as
a profit center. The organizational study would also review the effectiveness
of measures to reduce payroll costs. Among the actions currently conceived to
reduce these costs are retirement programs, retraining programs, and incentives
for employees.   Components of the staff redeployment plan are already under
implementation as noted in paras. 2.26 to 2.28. Assurances were obtained at
necotiations that PBDAC would rirovide the Bank not later than September 30, 1994.
with its annual retirement. retrainina and redeplovment orogram and. thereafter.
undate the Plan by April 30 of each vear: (paras. 3.25 and S.03(e)).
4.20        In order to rationalize and, as necessary, expand PBDAC's branch
network in the future, guidelines for decision making would be formulated, taking
into account the underlying objective of decentralization to the point of contact
with clients.
4.21        To enhance the level of knowledge of the rural economy and enlarge
banking activities to serve these areas, the PBDAC would organize a study of
rural financial market. The draft terms of reference of the study are contained
in Annex 2, Appendix 3. Local university researchers may be co-opted to carry
out this study in association with PEDAC staff. The rural savings study would
include recomendations for a medium-term strategy for deposit mobilization in
rural areas. Assurances were obtained at neaotiations that PBDAC would: (it
carry out a study of the rural financial market. with terms of reference
acceptable to the Bank. and provide the Bank with the findinas and
recommendations of the study not later than December 31. 1994: (ii} develop a
strateav for devosit mobilization and provide a plan and taroet for deposit
mobilization taking into account the findinas of rural financial study, to the
Bank not later than March 31. 1995 for review and comments: and (iii} establish
tarcets for deposit mobilization for each succeedina fiscal year durina the
oroiect implementation period and provide them to the Bank not later than March
31 of each year {para. 5.03(1)).
4.22        The PBDAC and the BDACs need to build up capacity to carry out
economic and financial analysis of projects, particularly for new types of
lending. It is also necessary to train the staff in appraising the environmental
risks associated with investments in sub-projects.   Guidelines governing
operations also need to be revised to incorporate environmental consideratioas
in project selection, appraisal, and monitoring. The Environmental Adviser in
the Office of the Vice-President of Operations of PBDAC would have overall
responsibility for monitoring the performance of the portfolio and would ensure
appropriate environmental standards being maintained in approving sub-projects.
The "twinning arrangement", as set out in para. 3.23, would provide for exchange
of visits and periodic involvement of experts from the "twin institution" to
directly guide the PBDAC staff. Necessary funding to facilitate such activities
is included in the project. Selection af the twin institution would be made by
the Project Director by December 31, 1994.
4.23        Provision of incentives is a necessary component in the operational
environment which prevails in Egypt. This is justified on grounds of poor civil
service pay scales and lack of differentiation between poor performers and high
achievers. Experience under bilateral programs indicates that a performance-
based incentive system would optimize benefits to the individuals concerned, and
in turn, to the institution and the economy at large. It is, therefore, proposed
to adapt PBDAC's existing incentive system to this project. Effectiveness of the
incentive system would be reviewed annually by the PMD as well as by Bank
supervision missions.



- 43 -
4.24        To  enable  PBDAC  to  successfully  carry  out  its  program  of
organizational strengthening while its operations are expanding and becoming more
diversified, technic I assistance would be nacessary, particularly in support of
planning and management, and loan appraisal and loan recovery. The critical part
of the project would be carried out through the end of 1996, which is an
appropriate time span for this essential support. Assurances were obtained at
negotiations on technical assistance arranoements for PBDAC {paras. 2.20, 4.25,
4.30 and 5.03(b)).
4.25        Technical assistance would be provided by two qualified specialists:
A development banking/planning specialist, who would be retained for a term of

would be a loan administration and recovery specialist, who would be retained for
a term of two years {paras. 2.20 and 5.03(b)).  Terms of reference for the
proposed specialists are given in Annex 2, Appendices 1 and 2.
4.26        The  financial  viability  of  PBDAC  in  the  changing  financial
environment will depend upon its ability to maintain an adequate interest margin
through intermediation of funds and transformation of assets and liabilities.
Assurances were obtained at neaotiations from PBDAC reaardino til recortina of

4.27        Since 1991, the Government has started divesting PBDAC of its input
supply and marketing functions. This is an important step in gearing PBDAC to
realize its rural banking functions. As the premier rural financial institution,
the PBDAC has a special role to play in providing leadership in this development.
With proper organization, it can provide investors with know-how in rural banking
and rural business opportunities. Under the project, P8DAC would, partly through
contracts with specialized agencies close to the banking sector, carry out
promotion and study activities and provide services to the 8DACs to help them
diversify their lending. These services would include the regular collection,
analysis and distribution of statistics on agricultural credit demand and supply,
and the dissemination of typical investment models that demonstrate their
technical, financial and economic viability, and the degree of risk involved.
In order to assess the impact of these activities and develop recommendations for
more efficient delivery of credit to the agricultural sector, the PBDAC would,
by the project's mid-term review, have in place an effective mechanism to
collect, analyze and disseminate data on agricultural credit supply and demand.
4.28        P8DAC will enhance its financial policy and management by better
utilizing existing information systems and by building up financial reporting
capabilities.    The organization has made progress  in computerizing loan
information, extending computer systems to BDAC, branch and village bank levels
(on a pilot basis), and in adopting standardized financial statements for several
levels in the PBDAC system. PBDAC is also working with a consultant group to
develop and operationalize a cost accounting system.  Plans are underway to
extend these initiatives throughout the PBDAC system, a process managed jointly
by PBDAC, donors and consultants.
4.29        PBDAC needs to develop accurate and concise financial reports for
management.   Many such reports may readily be developed using existing



- 44 -
information.  In certain cases, the timeliness, accura-y and completeness of
financial data may require attention. Because of the complexity of this task,
it is proposed that PBDAC initially focus attention on designing and creating a
handful of key reports.  The topics to be addressed are: key indicators of
financial performance, capacity to analyze variance in operating and monitoring
of asset quality, and recovery performance.
