P. Environmental INDact
4.31        Environmental priorities in Egypt are dominated by the critical need
to manage scarce water and land resources more efficiently. The project would
finance improved agricultural practices which would have environmental benefits
in terms of reducing use of: (a) water through laser land levelling and
conversion of flood irrigation to pressurized irrigation wherever appropriate;
(b) fertilizers through provision of equipment and services for fertilizer
drilling and fertigation; and (c) pesticides through intensified campaigns for
integrated pest management for cotton and other selected crops. Already, with
the removal of subsidies for fertilizers and pesticides, use of these inputs has
been reduced. Nonetheless, there is need for an environmental management plan
to ensure that mechanisms are put in place to prevent project funds being used
to finance activities that might have adverse environmental impacts.
4.32        The PBDAC does not possess environmental assessment skills at this
time. Although a special Environmental Unit was established within the MALR, the
staff of the unit does not have specific training or experience in environmental
audit or assessment. The Egyptian Environmental Affairs Agency (EEAA) is also
experiencing difficulty in finding national expertise for recruitment.   In-
country training programs are being developed with technical assistance from
external donors to increase the number of personnel trained in environmental
matters.



- 45 -
4.33        The environmental classification of the project is "8". The project
design provides for a four-pronged approach to deal with environmental concernst
(i) An Office of Environmental Adviser has been created in the PBDACI  A
qualified Technical Officer would be appointed to assist the Environmental
Adviser. To provide additional technical support to the Environmental Adviser,
an environmental specialist will be recruited internationally and financed under
the project for a term of two years. The Environmental Adviser with necessary
technical assistance, would draw up appropriate environmental guidelines for use
by the entire PBDAC network. The guidelines would identify types of sub-loans
which would require environmental analysis and would specify the extent of the
analysis required; (ii) Provision has been made for staff training in
env3 inmental screening, analysis and monitoring. Four training sessions of six
days, each would be held annually to familiarize the bank managers, credit
officers and loan supervision staff in these matters. In all, some 100
participants would benefit from these programs each year and a total of some 700
bank officials are expected to be trained during the project implementation
period; (iii) Sub-projects, particularly in agro-processing and land reclamation,
would require environmental analysis to be included in feasibility studies and
such analysis should include environmental mitigation plans as necessary. Sub-
projects with unacceptable environmental impacts would not be financed; and (iv)
Environmental training for SMSs, TT8s, extension workers and farmers is included
as part of the project. The curriculum for the training will be developed in
consultation with the Environmental Adviser and the EEAA. Assurances in this
reaard were obtained at neaotiations {paras. 4.13 and 5.03(j)).
4.34        In order to ensure the soundness of the project portfolio, the
Environmental Advisor would be appointed to the PSC. A representative of the
EEAA will serve on the IAC to ensure that the project activities are consistent
with the environmental laws and strategy of the country. The mid-term review of
the project would evaluate, anter alia, the progress made in implementing the
four items set forth above. Such early evaluation would facilitate adoption of
remedial measures as needed under the project.
G. Project Launching, Monitoring Evaluation and Reporting
4.35        A project launching workshop would be held in June/July 1994, with
the participation of the Project Director, the Project Coordinator, the Deputy
Directors, the key department heads of PBDAC, other relevant officials from MALR,
including heads of the three pilot RRESs. It would be followed by a separate
session to brief machinery importers and manufacturers and bankers interested in
participating in the project. These start-up missions would require staff input
of 12 staff weeks and would include four Bank staff members. The intensity of
the supervision offort would be greater during the early stages so as to ensure
smooth functioning of the project. The frequency of supervision would be, on an
average, twice a year in six monthly intervals. Annex 8 contains a proposed
supervision plan, with the details of the required staff inputs and skill
requirements.
4.36        PMD will monitor the physical and financial progress of the project,
in collaboration with the Evaluation and Follow-up Unit (EFU) of the Department
of Planning of PBDAC. It will collect and consolidate the data on sub-loan
commitments and disbursements, physical achievements under selected sub-projects,
operating results of investments financed, recovery performance, financial
indicators, subsidies, divestment of PBDAC's trading activities and related
issues of retirement of excess staff, staff training, construction of facilities,



- 46 -
and purchase of equipment and vehicles. PMD will provide: (i) to the management
of PBDAC and the PSC a summary monthly monitoring report, and (ii) to the Bank,
semi-annual progress reports within three months of the end of each semester
which will include commitments, disbursements, sub-loan maturities and actual
collections by category, status of training, staff reduction, and progress of
village bank construction, and procurement of equipment and vehicles. The semi-
annual reports would also cover a brief account of unusual problems encountered
in the process of implementation. Annual progress reports would contain sections
on the addition or deletion of dealers and custom hire operators, receptivity of
the farming community to new equipment demonstrations and installations, extent
of adoption of technology packages by the farming community, performance of TTSs
and extension workers, and agro-based enterprises established (number, types, and
distribution). An overall evaluation of project performance against targets set
in the annual plan would also be included in the report.  Specific sections
dealing with environmental issues encountered and resolutions thereof, and
support given to women and disadvantaged groups would be included in the report.
These reports would also evaluate progress made in the implementation of the
business plan, including the status of arrears calculated in a manner
satisfactory to the Bank.   In order to provide benchmarks to measure the
development impact of the project, three surveys would be carried out by the PMD,
one each in project year 1, 3 and 7 (Annex 9).
4.37        A mid-term review of the project will be carried out jointly by the
PMD and the Bank by December 1997. The review would, inter alia, cover: (i) the
progress made in the implementation plan resulting from PBDACs organization and
management study; (ii) progress made in PBDAC's Business Plan and performance
indicators set out in Annex 9 (the key indicators to be monitored would include
measures of profitability and efficiency of operations as indicated by key
ratios, progress in resource mobilization and in the implementation of the
training program); (iii) extent of commercial bank participation in the project;
(iv) effectiveness of the apex lending arrangement; (iv) effectiveness of the
technology transfer arrangements in the three pilot areas; and (v) effectiveness
of training given to PBDAC and MALR staff in environmental screening of projects,
and of technologies introduced.  Assurances to this effect were obtained at
egotiations {para. 5.03(q)).
4.38        Within six months of the closing date of the loan and credit, the PMD
will prepare a project completion report in the form proposed by the Bank.
B. Project Benefits and Risks
4.39        Project benefits would result in stronger agricultural institutions,
improved resource use, promotion of environmentally sound farming practices,
increased availability of mechanization support in all regions of the country,
more private sector participation in input and output trading, and enhanced
agricultural and agro-industrial support services in the pilot areas identified
in the project.   Increase in crop yields, reduction in farm input usage
(particularly in seed, fertilizer, pesticide and labor), reduction in post-
harvest losses, and increased value of products through processing would result
in more rural household incomes and a diversified rural economy. Direct benefits
yield an economic rate of return of about 38 per cent for the project (Annex 7).
4.40        Agricultural research and extension services would be brought closer
together at all levels through the project and there would be better information
exchange between the technical institutions and the credit institutions. The



- 47 -
benefits of such coordinated approach would assist various segments of the rural
community, including farmers and small rural entrepreneurs. The restructuring
of PBDAC into a full-fledged rural financial institution would improve rural
banking servicees besides, the support it would provide to traders, custom hire
operatore, and entrepreneurs in rural Egypt would stimulate investment and create
new job opportunities.
4.41        The benefits of institutional strengtheninig at the national level are
difficult to quantify. A conservative approach has been adopted in calculating
the direct benefits of the project. Details of the analysis are given in Annex
7.   Although on-farm technology improvement and farm related   enterprise
development complement each other, they have been analyzed separately to bring
into sharper focus their direct cost and benefit structures.
4.42        On-farm Technologv Improvement. It is assumed that the primazy means
of upgrading on-farm technology is through mechanization services. In Egypt, as
family holdings are generally small, machinery services are provided through
specialized custom hire operators and through neighboring farmer groups. Without
grouping together the beneficiaries, utilization of technologies such as laser
land-levelling would not be feasible. Based on prior experience of specialized
service providers, the analysis assumes a 10,000 fEddan model to capture the
benefits of using various types of equipment needed for modernization of farming.
The investment costs are based on retail prices for equipment prevailing in June
1993. Useful life of equipment was assumed as eight years. All economic values
were converted into local currency at LE 3.332 to one US dollar. Shadow wage
rate was equated with prevailing financial wage rate. A Standard Conversion
Factor (SCP) of 0.907 was applied toward the project's non-tradable costs and
benefits to adjust for remaining economic distortion. Adoption rate was assumed
to increase only gradually, rising from 30,000 feddans (or 0.5 per cent of the
planted area) in project year 1 to 480,000 feddans (or 6.0 per cent of the
planted area) in year 7 of the project. Despite the fact that mechanization and
improved farm.ng practices under the project would benefit all crops, benefits
were calculated only for those crops which lend themselves to row planting (e.g.,
wheat, barley, beans, sugar beet, flax, lentils, maize, rice, sorghum, soybeans,
peanuts, sesame and sunflower). While significant yield increases have been
observed in pilot operations, the project assumes yield increase of only 15 per
cent at full development; similarly, it assumes only a 10 per cent reduction in
input usage (such as, seed, fertilizer, and labor). No reduction in costs has
been assumed for usage of water and pumping, notwithstanding the fact that there
would be reduced need for water with improved land levelling.
4.43        World Bank projections were used to estimate economic prices of
traded inputs and outputs; financial prices were equated to economic prices in
the case of non-traded goods. A time lag of six months was assumed between the
time of investment in equipment and the yield benefit to the farmer.
4.44        The resulting Economic Rate of Return (ERR) of the on-farm technology
component is 38 per cent, and the Financial Rate of Return (FRR) is 27 per cent.
The reduced FRR is attributable mainly to the inclusion of 1S per cent of taxes
and import duties. At full development, this component would result in an
incremental crop production benefit of about LE 82 million and saving of about
LE 2 million worth of seeds and about LB 4 million worth of fertilizers per
annum. While 7,500 unskilled jobs would be lost, there would be an offsetting
gain of 5,100 skilled jobs in farm machinery operations.   Additional job
opportunities would be created in machinery repair services and in product
handling and marketing networks, but no estimates have been made of these



- 48 -
numbers. In all, the on-farm technology component would benefit some 240,000
farm families.
4.45        Sensitivity Analysis.  A shadow discount rate of 13.5 per cent has
been assumed as the cut-off rate for optimal utilization of investment capital.

4.46        Benefit calculations assume a relatively low adoption rate and
conservative increment in yields of 15 per cent, notwithstanding results
indicated by farm surveys which indicate more than 20 per cent yield increases.
There is, therefore, adequate safeguard built into the assumption of total
benefits. As regards costs, the prices of farm machinery and equipment are
likely to remain stable, given the high degree of competition in the Xgyptian
market among dealers of various international machinery and equipment and the
competition which exists among the fast growing custom hire service contractors.
4.47        Aaro-based Enterorises.  The liberalized economic environment in
Egypt is conducive to the growth of a new class of rural entrepreneurs. Recent
experience under the ongoing Second Agricultural Development Project (Ln. 2561-
EGT) indicates that there is considerable scope for expanding rural private
investments, provided the needed technical, financial and market intelligence
services could be brought within the reach of such entrepreneurs. It is not
feasible to predict the pattern of investment demand for the numerous types of
agro-based enterprises. For purposes of this project analysis, four prototypes
of rural-based enterprises (i.e. Emall-scale custom service operation, medium-
scale custom service operation, small-scale rice and wheat milling, and medium-
scale horticultural sorting and packing station) have been selected.   The
analysis includes all costs, including working capital needs.   The average
economic life assumed varies between 8 and 10 years.  For economic analysis
purposes, direct transfer payments, such as, taxes and duties have been deducted
from financial prices and a SCF of 0.907 was applied toward the non-tradable
costs and benefits of individual enterprises. The ERR and PRR calculations for
these enterprises are presented in Annex 7, Tables 1-16 and are summarized belows

4.49        Proiect Risks.  The project faces three significant risks:  First,
slackening or reversal of macroeconomic reform is a risk which would discourage
investors.   While the current slackness in investment growth seems to be
attributable to economic recession and weak demand, effective implementation of
the private sector development and public sector reform aspects of ERSAP would
be of critical importance for the project's success.   Second,  delays in
implementation of the redeployment program may occur due to inadequacy of
resources for PBDAC to make severance payments for its redundant staff. The
project provides for retraining so that the displaced employees could be
redeployed to other activities, such as trading and other professions in the
service sector. Third, participation of commercial banks in onlending to the
rural economy which is one of the project's objectives, may be at risk if it
develops only slowly. Provision of technical assistance and training under the
project should help reduce this risk.

ARAB REPUBLIC OF EGYPT
AGRICULTURAL MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Agricultural Sector Developments
A. Agriculture in the EgvDtian Economy
1.          The Egyptian economy has traditionally relied on the agriculture
sector as a source for growth, both in terms of contribution to GDP and
employment. This central role was reinforced by the strong performance of the
sector initiated by the reforms following the 1952 revolution, and further
supported by the completion of the High Aswan Dam in 1968, which allowed for a
significant increase in the cultivated area and provided assured perennial
irrigation to the entire Nile Valley and Delta areas.
2.          Mainly due to inappropriate macroeconomic and trade policies during
1970s, agricultural GDP growth which was 3.5 percent in the 1970s fell to 2.5
percent in the 1980s; and agriculture's role in national employment declined from
almost 50% to 35% during this period. The declining share of the sector in GDP
is also attributable to the relatively stronger growth in other sectors
(particularly,  oil,  and  also  services  and  construction).    The  sector,
nevertheless, remains important for the future growth of the economys
horticultural production having increased significantly over the last few years,
the sector continues to support agro-industrial growth in the country; the sector
is expected to lead the Government's efforts to achieve equitable and balanced
growth in the rural economy under recently adopted liberalization efforts.
3.          The modest overall performance during the last decade can be
attributed to policy distortions, disincentives inherent to a system of
administered prices, and the declining share of agriculture in total public
investments during the last 25 years. After separating out the cost of the High
Dam, agriculture's share of public investment declined from 14% in 1962-66, to
only 7% during the second plan period (1988-92). Furthermore, the Government
emphasized land reclamation programs within the agriculture sector, with a
concomitant bias towards it in the allocation of investment funds (about half of
the total funds earmarked for the agriculture sector were allocated to horizontal
expansion); however, despite the fact that these investments have so far failed
to generate returns on the scale expected from it.
4.           Since the mid-80s, the sector has clearly been at the forefront of
policy and institutional reforms.  Most of the reforms (particularly those
relating to crop area allocations, pricing, trading, etc.) have been carried out;
other institutional reforms (such as land tenure arrangements, creation of a
commodity exchange for cotton, etc.) are underway. Already, the gap between
domestic and world market prices for most commodities has been closed. Due to
the concurrent sharp decline in world market prices during this period, real



- 53 -
Page 2 of 18
domestic prices remained largely stable.   So far, the main benefit from
liberalization has been that farmers could flexibly respond to such yield to
maximize their benefit and income by changing cropping patterns as evidenced in
area increases of 58%, 12% and 4% for wheat, rice and maize respectively from
1985-90 (Table 1). The preliminary statistical data for 1992 shows a further
gain in area and yield in rice and wheat. Liberalization measures reinforced
technological progress in production and was instrumental in increasing wheat
production by 134%, rice by 28%, and maize by 34% over the same five-year period.
As a result, imports of wheat and wheat flour have declined in recent years, and
maize imports remained largely unchanged since the mid-1980s, in spite of a
rapidly growing population. At the same time, the berseem (clover) area declined
by 16% resulting from the relatively reduced profitability of livestock. Also,
these policy shifts witnessed an expansion in production of horticultural crops.

B. Resource Biase
5.          On a per caput basis, Egypt's area of cultivable land at 0.13 feddan
per head is among the lowest in the world. Also, agriculture is almost entirely
dependent on irrigation from the Nile river, as the country has no effective
rainfall except in a narrow band along the northern coastal areas.   The
availability of reliable water supply from the High Aswan Dam is governed by the
existing water sharing agreement with Sudan, under which 55.5 BCM are allocated
to Egypt; availability of water represents the ultimate limiting factor on the
country's ability to expand agriculture horizontally. The pressing need is to
maximize the returns to water in an environmentally sound manner.   Still
potentially suitable soils for further development outside of the already



- 54 -
ANNE I
Page 3 of 18
cultivated areas do exist in Egypt; however, the ability of the nation to tap
this potential is wholly governed by the limitations on the water resources.
6.          The agricultural land base of Egypt totalling about 7.5 million
feddans, consists of some 7.3 million feddans (about 3.0 million ha) lying within
the Nile basin and delta, and about 200,000 feddans (80,000 ha) of agricultural
land elsewhere (rainfed and in oases). Of the total area in the Nile basin and
delta, some 5.4 million fed are "old" lands, and the remaining 1.9 million fed
are "new" lands, reclaimed since 1952. The NALR statistics shows that: (i) the
cultivated land increased by almost one million fed between 1982 and 1990; (ii)
the land under orchard almost doubled during this period; (iii) the number of
holdings increased by 18%; and (iv) the landless farmers increased more than that
of landholders, which indicates that land is increasingly operated by contract
and tenant farmers. However, with the continuing loss of agricultural lands to
village/urban encroachment, and given the limitation of water availability for
irrigated agriculture, the potential for significantly increasing the available
agricultural land base from its present level is limited. Consequently, future
growth in agricultural production will need to come from more efficient
utilization of the existing water and land resources.
7.          Egypt's agricultural land is, for the most part, highly productive
and ideally suited to intensive agriculture. With good climatic conditions,
including maximum sunlight and a perennial source of irrigation water,
agriculture is provided with excellent growing conditions, resulting in high crop
yields. The "Old Land" has generally fertile clay soils of recent alluvial
origin. New Lands (including Old New Lands) which comprise of reclaimed area
mostly calcarious sandy loam to silty clay loam soils with relatively low
inorganic content and micro-nutrients. Cropping intensities in the Old Land are
very high, at 200%, compared to the New Land intensities of around 150%. In
spite of intensive cultivation, potential exists to further increase agricultural
production and productivity through appropriate modernization schemes.
C. Farming Systems
8.          Clear variations in cropping pattern exist from the Delta to Upper
Egypt and within any given zone; the most significant difference is between the
Old Land and New Land. There are three cropping seasonss Winter (November to
May), Summer (April/May to October), and Nili (July/August to October). In the
Old Land, an elaborate crop rotation system is followed, the most representative
of which is shown in Figure 1. Rotations generally last three years and are
organized according to growing seasons. Most crops are grown both in the Delta
and Valley, with the exception of rice (Delta mainly) and sugar cane (Valley).
In the New Land, groundnut (sandy soils) and fruits and vegetables, are
particularly important. Table 1 shows that deregulation of crop area and price
controls has resulted in significant changes in the crop mix.
9.          The main winter crons are berseem, wheat and faba beans.  8erseem is
the major fodder crop, grown either over three months with only 2 cuts, as a soil
improver before cotton (short berseem), or over 6-7 months, either with 4-5 cuts



- 55 -
Page 4 of 18
or grazed by tethered cattle (long berseem). After rice, long berseem is dibbled
in the stubble, which avoids land preparation and extends the crop duration.
Wheat is a major winter crop and the area under wheat has been steadily rising
in recent years. Other minor winter crops are: barley, sugar beet, flax and
various grain legumes or pulses.
10.         Among the summer crops maize or sorghum, rice and cotton are
dominant. Maize is grown both as a staple and for fodder; it is replaced by
sorghum in the hotter areas of Upper Egypt. Rice is grown exclusively in the
Delta and some in Fayoum and is all transplanted. It is particularly grown in
the coastal areas. Cotton is the most important export crop of Egypt. Long and
extra-long staple varieties are grown, in which Egypt enjoys a dominant position
in world markets.  Due to low procurement prices, areas under cotton have
decreased from 1.6 m fed in 1970 to 0.8 m fed in 1992. Other, minor summer crops
are: soya beans, peanuts, sesame and sunflower.
11.         Other crops, such as tomatoes, potatoes, onions and garlic are
cultivated for most part in the year. Citrus and potatoes are successful export
crops; some vegetables are also exported. However, export is weakly organized;
while the European community is the most important market for cotton, rice and
vegetable exports, the East European countries have absorbed the bulk of citrus
exports.
12.         Yields  have  continually  expanded  during  the  past  decades,
particularly for wheat, maize and vegetables, through improvements in irrigation
and drainage, selection of variety and input use. Yields per hectare from the
old Lands are quite high by international standards, even when compared with
those of developed countries, as shown below:

14.         The low cropping intensity in the New Lands is partly attributable
to water shortages, which also affect yields. Other reasons include:
(1)   the lack of labor, resulting in insufficient crop care,
particularly weeding.  This affects chiefly graduates, who
depend mainly on hired labor as families are limited in size
or live in cities.
(2)   the lack of resources for recent settlers, compounded by
shortage of credit availability.    This translates  into
insufficient input use, particularly fertilizers.
Status of Crop Production and Agro-Processin_ Technoloties
15.         For sustained development, Egyptian agriculture needs continued
upgrading of its farm implements and adoption of proven technology. Pilot trials
in Egypt have shown that through the usage of appropriate technology in the
field, substantial increases in crop yield was realized with significant
reduction in the application of crop inputs. Recent poliries of free trade and
reduced market distortions have improved access to modern technology. This has
to be supported by a coordinated effort to upgrade technical advisory services
at the farm level, and to broaden the scope and depth of rural credit coverage
in order to quicken the pace of agricultural modernization and to increase
yields.



- 57 -
ANN
Page 6 of 18
D. Agricultural Support Services
16.        A_ricultural Research.  The Agricultural Research Center (ARC) of
MALR is the primary agency responsible for technology generation. ARC is headed
by a Director General who reports to the Minister through a board of directors,
and its mandate covers research both in crops and livestock. ARC consists of 17
research institutes, six central laboratories and ten Regional Research and
Extension Stations (RRESs) and 32 secondary research stations.  Research is
organized on a commodity basis with separate institutes for field crops,
horticulture,  cotton,  sugar,  livestock production and animal health.   In
addition, ARC has separate institutes to cover the following disciplines and
extension related services: soil and water, plant protection, plant pathology,
serum and vaccines, mechanization, and agricultural economics. Within MPWWR,
there is a Water Research Center, which comprises of 11 institutes and a training
center, that undertakes research in water resources/irrigation and drainage
aspects related to agriculture. In addition, the National Academy of Scientific
Research and Technology and National Research Center, under the Ministry of
Scientific Research and Technology, are also involved in agricultural research
through their specialized irrigation, food and agriculture divisions.   The
private sector is becoming involved in a limited way in agricultural research,
dealing with seeds, tissue culture, agro-chemicals and farm machineries.
Agricultural faculties in universities are also an essential part of the research
network.
17.         Recently, a major policy decision was taken to decentralize extension
and research activities by expanding the role of the RRESs and to strengthen
liaison and communication between the central research institutes and RRESs.
Accordingly, so far, seven centers have been upgraded and mobilized, each serving
two or more governorates.   Each regional station has its own director, a
coordinating board comprising of representatives from research, local
universities, agricultural and extension departments of the concerned
governorate, and some farmer leaders. The research activities in the stations
are largely based on plans provided by the research institute at the headquarter.
As such, the regional stations have limited flexibility to pursue independent
programs focused on regional stations problems. ARC has developed a number of
technology packages particularly in cereals, and some horticu'tural crops which
have been adopted by farmers with significant positive impact on crop yields.
Until now, research emphasis has largely been on traditional crops in the Nile
Delta and the Nile Valley (Old Lands), and limited attention has been paid to
provide suitable technologies to different categories of farmers in the reclaimed
New Lands or in dry lands.
18.         While Egypt has a large research network and staffed with qualified
scientists, its major weaknesses stem from overlapping responsibilities among
various institutions, lack of sufficient coordination between research and
extension, inadequate focus on production problems of different categories of
farmers, lack of economic assessment of various technical recommendations, and
shortage of support and operational funds. There is also a need for effective
decentralization of research in order to encourage regional stations to assume
more active role in planning research - extension programs in their regions.



- 58 -
ANN    I
Page 7 of 18
Agricultural research projects such as the Egyptian Major Cereal Improvement
Program (ENCIP), Rice Research and Training, Agricultural Development Systems,
Egyptian Water and Management Project, Agricultural Mechanization, Small Farmer
Production Program, Agricultural Production and Credit Project, the Irrigation
Management Systems Project and the National Agricultural Research Project,
supported by the USAID have been assisting ARC in addressing some of these issues
by developing multi-disciplinary research teams, reorienting and improving
facilities for on-farm research, and strengthening the Technology Transfer
Component (TTC) in order to establish a strong two-way links between research-
exteneion and farmers. At this stage of development, on-farm research has to be
supported to strengthen the technology generation capabilities of the reglonal
stations to enable them to produce menus of recommendations from which farmers
could choose most desirable ones. Following is a brief review of the research
institutes that the agricultural modernization project would depend on.
19.         The hgriculIural Entineering Research Institute (AERI) is the main
government organization responsible for conducting research in all phases of
agricultural machinery development, energy conservation, and the adaptation of
new technologies. The AERI has the added responsibility of evaluating newly-
developed and imported machinery and must evaluate the sultability of those
machines for use in the Egyptian farming sector.   In addition, two new
departments, one department for on farm-level water management and another for
agro-processing, were created in AERI in 1992; however, it will take sometime
before these departments become fully operational.   AERI's most significant
research activities at the present time focus on the development of rice
threshing and related harvesting equipment. These machines, are ideally suited
for local manufacture and will provide farmers with a practical solution to their
harvesting constraints.   AERI gencrally disseminates its findings through
establishing exhibitions, displays and demonstrations during field days.
20.         The Soil and Water Research Institute is dealing with research work
on plant nutrition, soil physical and chemical properties, irrigation agronomy,
and drainage. Some of the major research developments relating to on-farm water
management have been estimation of crop water requirements in principal crops,
study of quality of irrigation and drainage water, and the efficiency of reusing
drainage water in irrigation.
21.         The Agricultural Economics Research Institute has eight divisions and
85 senLor researchers. The research conducted by Production Economics, Commodity
Analysis, and Marketing Divisions have direct relevance to the kinds of price and
marketing information that the project intends to disseminate among farmers and
investors. These researches deal with cost of production in crops, the crop
supply and demand outlook, commodity prices forecast, and issues dealing with
agro-processing costs.
22.         The Food and Nutrition Research Institute has 160 researchers and 200
technicians. It is well equipped and has good pilot plant facilities that are
ideal for both product development and training. However, much of the pilot
plant equipment is too large for small enterprises and the institute is in need
of smaller, lower cost, alternatives more suited to the needs of the small scale
rural investors and micro-enterprises.



- 59 -
ANNEX-1
Page 8 of 18
23.         Agricultural Extension.  Agricultural extension at the national level
is directed by the Central Administration of Extension in the MALR. It is headed
by a First Undersecretary, who is assisted by an undersecretary and two
coordinators,  one in the Delta and one in Upper Egypt regions.   At the
governorate level, there are two functionaries: the Director of Agrarian Reform
Extension is serving farmers within area of his responsibility, and the Director
of Extension responsible for non-agrarian reform areas. Also, QARPAD provides
limited extension services for newly-reclaimed land. Several divisions of MALR,
particularly Pest Control, Horticulture, and Animal Production, have large
numbers of field staff who are also partially engaged in extension activities.
Generally, extension specialists for field crops, livestock, horticulture and
training are deployed in each district to supervise and support the Village
Extension Workers (VEW). At the village level, the VEW is assisted by two or
more extension assistants, generally one for each 200 fed.  Although, some
overlap of functions takes place among technical staff in the governorates, the
results of some pilot projects show that the major role for technology transfer
is played by the Technology Transfer Specialists (TTSs). They are trained by a
Subject Matter Specialist (S4S), stationed in the RRES.  TTSs work in close
collaboration with SMSs, and VEWs, and provide a two-way flow of information.
24.         Linkage between extension and research is in need of substantial
strengthening. It exists chiefly at the level of the RRES, and the Director of
Extension at the governorate level is a member of the Coordinating Board of the
RRES. However, in the chain of regional and village extension network, extension
support for agricultural mechanization and farm-level irrigation are particularly
weak and under-represented; no structure exists within the extension system to
provide intelligence report on marketing and output prices; and the agricultural
support services are much weaker in the New Land where services are generally
limited to specific areas covered by a few donor-financed projects.
E. Agricultural Farm Inputs
25.         Fertilizers.  Egyptian soils are poor in organic matter content and
available nitrogen, but contain average amounts of phosphorous and potassium; the
sandy and calcareous soils, particularly on the New Lands, are deficient in macro
and micro nutrients.   Some of these problems have been compounded by the
discontinuation of silt deposits after the construction of the high dam.
Therefore, there is relatively high fertilizer application under intensive
agriculture. Since 1990, subsidy on fertilizer has been gradually phased out and
at present, fertilizer is sold at market prices and more than 90% of fertilizer
is traded through private channels. The volume of fertilizer sale has dropped,
mainly due to more than three fold price rise during this period as a result of
elimination of fertilizer subsidies.
26.         Egypt has developed a fertilizer industry that can provide most of
its N and P requirements. Potassium fertilizers, which are imported, are seldom
used (9,000 t K20 in 1988), except on sandy and calcareous soils. Fertilizers
which are produced locally are in outmoded form, mainly flakes and run-of-pile
TSP and SSP, and distributed through broadcasting. The fertilizer technology



- 60 -
Page 9 of Is
used in Egypt is not keeping pace with the developments in developed countries.
This technological deficiency was recognized by UNIDO in 1985, resulting in the
establishment of the Egyptian Fertilizer Development Center (EFDC), with the
assistance of IFDC, Nuscle Shoals, Alabama.   Through EFDC, the   Egyptian
fertilizer industry would make available to the farmer a more diversified and
sophisticated choice of fertilizer types to help meet the demand for more
specialized fertilizers, while maximizing use of indigenous materials.   In
particular, the physical properties of the products would be improved to make
them more suitable for mechanical application and to prepare products tailored
to provide nutrients in a more timely basis in relation to the plant growth
needs. Combinations of nutrients (major and micro) will be developed which will
have definite advantages over the use of individual nutrients.
27.         Seeds.  Egypt's seed industry is well established and supported by
competent research. ARC is involved in developing/introducing of improved high
yielding varieties (HYVs), including production of breeder and foundation seed.
The Central Administration of Seeds (CAS) in MALR is also engaged in some seed
multiplication, treatment and distribution, as well as doing regulatory
functions; the Egyptian Agriculture Organization imports foreign seed, and also
owns seed cleaning and conditioning facilities which it makes available to the
private sector; the organization for Improvement of Egyptian Cotton supervise
cotton seed; and six private sector seed companies are involved in corn,
vegetable and forage crops seed production. Egypt also exports seeds, some 8,600
tons in 1989, but mainly clover from farmers' fields and yellow flint maize.
There are several new varieties of corn, wheat, soybean and rice in the research
pipeline with potentials for higher yields and shorter maturity time.
28.         In line with liberalization policy, the government has begun to
privatize seed multiplication industry and has lifted the government subsidy on
most of the seed. As such, the prices of seeds offered through government-
sponsored channels would rise and greater participation of private sector in seed
production and trade is expected in the future.   At the same time, the
government's responsibility in seed quality control functions would be
strengthened.
29.         Pesticides. Imports of pesticides which amounted to more than 30,000
tons per year in the mid-80s has dropped to less than 9,500 tons in crent years.
Pesticides applied to cotton represented the largest portion of imported
pesticides; the rest to rice, wheat, sorghum and fruits and vegetables. All
pesticides and active ingredients for local formulations are imported by some 30
licensed public and private companies. Repacking or formulation is done in five
public and one private enterprise registered with MALR and the Ministry of
Industry. A total of 350 pesticides have been approved by MALR for use in the
country. Following liberalization measures in 1989/90, any company could engage
in pesticide distribution if it is licensed by MALR and can provide professional
advice in all its retailing outlets by a hired qualified agronomist (BSc level).
The Central Laboratory for pesticides is responsible for control on quality of
all pesticides.



- 61 -
AM=I
Page 10 of 18
30.         To address the environmental concern regarding this, HADR, with the
assistance of some donors, such as GTZ, encourages a program of integrated peat
management, where the focus needs to be on interrupting the pest life cycle, use
of modern biological pest control, adoption of improved cultural practices and
judicious use of pesticides. These efforts, combined with the phasing out of
pesticide subsidy, have resulted in a steep decline in pesticide usage.
31.        Labor. Egypt would appear to be a classic case of a country with a
labor surplus. However, the rapid economic growth rate of the Middle East region
has influenced several closely-related changes in the workforce of rural Egypt,
particularly during the 1970s: (i) Unskilled laborers have joined the better
trained workers in emigrating to the neighboring oil countries to find work; (ii)
Laborers have left the rural areas to find more desirable employment in the
service and construction industries in the cities; (iii) The increase in
education programs in the rural areas has provided opportunities for graduates
to seek government employment in the population centers. A "labor shortage" was
claimed by the Government policymakers and farmers as they encountere'd peak labor
demands which could not be satisfied. To a certain level this labor shortage
stimulated agricultural mechanization across Egypt as tractcr power was use' for
tillage, threshing and irrigation to replace traditional animal draft technology
and manual labor. In spite of high degree of primary mechanization across Egypt,
the scarcity of farm labor is real as labor demand peaks during May-June and
September-October each year, when harvest, land preparation and planting follow
each other in continuous succession. During these periods, a labor shortage
exists in all farming areas and maintaining proper timeliness for field
operations becomes nearly impossible.
32.         flFjbjSr-.  Mechanization of agriculture expanded rapidly during
1970s.  Already in 1982, over 90% of land preparation was estimated to be
mechanized. There are about 40,000 tractors in Egypt and most of them between
50 to 70 h.p. The local NASOO tractor, produced under a license agreement with
Yugoslavia, has provided a cheap source of power; however, it has been affected
recently by the interruption of component supply from Yugoslavia. While tractor
and machinery from western countries is found to be too expensive for the
Egyptian market, equipment from various other sources including China, Russia and
Belarus, is becoming available at moderate prices. There is a growing market for
equipment.
33.        Egypt has quite an impressive variety of primary and secondary
equipment in its machinery inventory list (Table 1). Based on ABERI's estimate,
the demand for agricultural machinery in the next seven years iould expand by
about 10 percent per annum and the cost of this expansion plus replacing existing
machineries at the end of their service life (estimated to be 10 years) would
amount to about LE 832 million, in 1992 constant prices, per annum. Although
very few farmers own their machinery, most of those who own tractors provide
custom services to neighbors. The private custom hiring services, business is
increasing as liberalization in the economy has taken roots. The private custom
hiring services is supported by small village repair and maintenance workshops,
which provide fairly efficient services for primary mechanization equipment (land
preparation, transport, water lifting and threshing). The MALE operates some 90



- 62 -
ANNEX I
Page 11 of 18
mechanization centers and in addition AERI manages 8 mechanical centers which
also provide limited renting services. However, the publicly-sponsored services
are reducing rapidly as their fleet is ageing and as the agencies are pressured
to be cost effective and compete with more efficient private entrepreneurs in the
market.
34.         While  the  present  mechanization  system  offers  a  relatively
satisfactory level of basic services, it does not respond to the increasingly
demanding needs of Egyptian agriculture.   The choice of the implements for
primary mechanization is limited, and secondary mechanization which, beyond
relieving labor shortages at peak times, could improve yields, is progressing
slowly. The Second Agricultural Development Project (Ln. 2561-EGT) succeeded in
opening up trading channels and private custom hire services to expand usage of
secondary and more versatile farm level technology; however, substantial
additional investments are deemed essential to ensure growth and expansion of
private entrepreneurs in custom service operation. To enhance their success rate
provision of training support, upgrading of extension messages and assistance to
farmers in making an educated choice in the selection and usage of appropriate
equipment are also needed.
35.         On-farm Water Use. Excessive use of water by farmers leads to water
logging which results in decreased yields, soil degradation, and increase
drainage problems. Therefore, water conservation and achievement of high on-farm
water use efficiency are major strategic objectives. However, presently, the
extension service has limited activities in these fields.
36.         In the Old Lands, mainly flood irrigation of small flat or furrowed
basins is practiced. Basins are of various sizes which are separated by dikes
or shallow surface drains and are generally not well levelled resulting in
overflooding of some parts, and substantial on-farm water losses.  Also some
losses occur between canals, which are generally unlined, and particularly in
basins lmasaasl, and farm gates. The main thrust of the project activities will
be on promoting precision land levelling with lasers.  It is reported that
levelling with lesser reduced water losses by 10-20% and increased yields by 10-
30% in limited areas where it has been practiced. Also, emphasis will be laid
on helping farmers improve basin design and irrigation scheduling, as well as
their knowledge and understanding of good on-farm water management practices.
In addition, farmers would be encouraged to form Water Users Association (WUA)
and undertake joint activities in improvement and maintenance of masaas.
Initiatives in this regard have been undertaken by the Ministry of Public Works
and Water Resources under the USAID--supported Irrigation Management Systems
Project. Extension activities will be needed to promote the use of pressurized
irrigation systems in the horticultural areas.
37.         In New Lands, although some flood irrigation is still practiced (Old
New Land), use of pressurized irrigation is more common.   These include
sprinklers, used in irrigation of traditional crops such as wheat, alfalfa, and
micro-irrigation used mainly for orchards and vegetable production. The major
problems encountered by the farmers in New Lands are unreliability of water



- 63 -
ANNEX I
Page 12 of 18
supply, some drainage problems, lack of credit, and inadequate training of
settlers in handling of irrigation equipment. Under the project, main emphasis
will be laid on training of settlers in the proper use and maintenance of
existing irrigation equipment, promoting installation of new units, encouraging
the use of fertigation where feasible, and improving access to credit. Special
attention will be paid to train a cadre of Technology Transfer Specialists (TTS)
in on-farm irrigation. Also research-extension capability in irrigation agronomy
would be strengthened by sending selected senior staff abroad for short-term
training.
38.         Market Information. Wholesale markets, and marketing operations are
generally not well developed in Egypt. Particularly, information on demand for
various agricultural crops for internal consumption and exports and projections
on production and market prices for outputs and inputs are inadequate. In the
absence of any guidance to farmers, they are unable to introduce changes in their
cropping systems in response to internal and external market needs. As a result,
they suffer sometimes from over-production (or lack of markets), resulting in
heavy post-harvest losses particularly in perishable crops.   For certain
horticultural crops, losses as high as 35% have been reported.   Under the
project, the Agricultural Economics Research Institute would be assigned the
responsibility to develop market information about agricultural products and
inputs, which will be passed on to the research-extension system, in a usable
form on a regular basis so that it could be disseminated to the farmers. For
this purpose, financing has been provided in the project to enable the institute
to strengthen their capacity in this field.  Also, training abroad will be
provided to two specialists to help adopt an effective program to collect and
disseminate market information in a timely manner. It is expected that market
information would help farmers to make more intelligent decisions regarding
choice of crops, area to be cultivated, and investments to be made, and thus
enable them to respond to market conditions. Also, market information will be
made accessible to private traders and agro-processors as required.
F. Agro-Based Industry Development
39.         Agricultural marketing channels in Egypt aret (i) traditional local
markets for products such as fruit and vegetable, milk, forage, pulses, and live
animals; and (ii) centrally organized supply channels for other products such as
cotton, rice, wheat, cane sugar. Food processing activities, including wheat and
rice milling, oilseeds crushing, cotton ginning, cane sugar extracting, and
starch processing are largely handled by State-owned enterprises. Few processing
factories have their own collection network. Allocation of imported raw or semi-
processed agricultural products to these factories is mostly handled by the
Ministry of Supply and Home Trade and the Ministry of Industry. State controlled
domestic marketing channels have long played a major role with mainly government
controlled cooperatives and PEDAC supplying enterprises.
40.         With the exception of cane sugar, Egyptian farmers have now the
freedom of choosing their trade channels. The pricing system for their products
is also benefiting from a major shift from purely administered pricing, to a
liberal pricing system. Already investment in agro-related industries has been



64 -
Page 13 of 18
thriving, and lending volume in this sector has more than doubled in the last two
years.
41.         At present,  little or no direct mechanism exists,  to provide
technical support or training to the rural-based small- and medium-scale agro-
enterprise sector. The Food and Nutrition Research Institute (FNRI) of ARC
concentrates on applied research and development and has developed a number of
interesting products and processing equipment that could find application in
small scale plants.  However, FNRI does not have the institutional capacity to
disseminate its recommendations to investors. There are some 15 Universities in
Egypt that offer food science training.  These universities, like FNRI, have an
interesting portfolio of products that offer investment potential; however, so
far there are no channels to translate these findings into concrete investment
schemes. The Food Industries Corporation which acts as the holding company for
some 19 large parastatal food manufacturing companies does not have a direct role
in assisting small producers; nor does it have laboratories or other facilities
of its own.  Similarly, the extension service of MALR is yet to expand its
service to cover promotion of agro-based industries in Egypt; so far, its
function has been limited to providing some home economic services.
42.      A large number of small- and medium-scale farm-related enterprises are
in operation in Egypt. Most of them, however, developed on traditional methods
without much institutionalized technical support. Small farmers have for long
delivered part of their wheat and rice production to small local private millers.
There are about 3,700 small wheat mills. These are generally stone mills carved
in Egyptian quarries, and about 80% of them use diesel engines.  Flour and
semolina are used by two major types of industries: (i) the baking industry, with
a private ownership of about 85%; and (ii) the macaroni industry, with a private
ownership of more than 50% of the processing capacity. There are a number of
small privately-owned dairy, fruit and vegetable, and meat processing facilities.
With the liberalization of marketing and processing facilities, it is expected
that the demand for these small farm-related activities such as feedmills, rice
and wheat mills, packing houses, bakeries, dairy plants, farm supplies and
service depots, transportation and machinery services, and machine shops would
expand.
43.         In Egypt all enterprises have to register, and if foods are involved,
they have to obtain a Public Health Certificate also. Although, the regulations
for domestically consumed products are loosely applied, consumer safety is
becoming a matter of increasing concern. However, any products aimed at export
markets  have  to  meet  increasingly  stringent    standards  with  producers
demonstrating that "due diligence" procedures are in place.
0. Technical Components of the Project
44.         The fundamental objective of this component of the project is to
increase crop yields and enhance rural income through strengthening technical
advisory services in farm machinery, on-farm irrigation and water management,
market information and intelligence, and agro-based itdustries. In these
r



- 65 -
Page 14 of 18
specified areas, a network would be established to disseminate proven
technologies to the farmers and investors and to enhance investors technical and
marketing knowledge to enable them to make an informed decision on the type of
investment and choice of cropping pattern. In pursuit of this objective, on a
pilot basis, the project will work with three RRESs and their connected
governorate extension system to conduct its envisaged program. In these centers,
a subject matter specialist (SMS) in each of the four technical areas (i.e., on-
farm irrigation, agricultural mechanization, agro-industry and economics and
market information), will be identified to work with selected technology transfer
specialists (TTS). SMSs and TTSs would be given appropriate training both on-
the-job and in local training centers.  They would be provided office supplies,
transportation facilities, and incentive allowance to enable them to carry out
their duties. Similarly, Agro-Enterprise and the PBDAC's staff capacity will be
strengthened to facilitate credit services for medium and long-term investments
in agro-enterprise financing and on-farm investment promotion.
45.         The project's strategy is to promote utilization by small farmers of
proven technology, regardless of its source of origin. A hypothetical 10,000 fed
model for a selected number of field crops (Table 4) has been chosen for
contractors of custom machinery services to serve the small farmers. The package
includes use of additional farm equipment shown in Table 5. Based on research
findings and mission conclusions, adoption of the technologies specified in the
model would result in crop yield increases of at least 15%, and reduce the
present input requirement for seed and fertilizers by at least 10%.
46.         The SMSs for the project will be drawn from the concerned institutes
of the ARC, namely, Agricultural Engineering Research Institute (AERI),
Agricultural Economics Research Institute (AECRI), Soil and Water Research
Institute (SWRI), and Food Technology and Nutrition Research Institute (FNRI).
They will work full time in the project. The staff will be selected based on
qualifications, relevant experience, and commitment to the program. Through the
project, new office space will be constructed in Sakha and Nubariya to house the
project staff; office equipment and furniture would be provided in all three
centers. As the center in Nubariya is in a poor state (laboratories without
equipment, buildings with major repair and rehabilitation needs, and research
staff without living quarters), the project would provide funds for
rehabilitating the center and will finance purchase of equipment for its soil and
water and pesticide laboratories.   In addition,  in this station a small
irrigation demonstration farm will be established.
47.         The main strategy for dissemination of improved technology under the
project would be taken through Extension Information and Demonstration Units
(EIDU).    Each EIDU is designed to serve 10,000 farmers and will have one
extensionist, and technology transfer specialists (TTS), one each in farm
mechanization, on-farm water management, agro-processing, and market information.
All these staff will be drawn from the existing cadre of extension services in
the governorate, and they will be carefully selected in order to ensure the
success of the program.   Minimum qualifications will be B.S.  degree in
agriculture. The extensionist, who will be the head of the EIDU, should have
long field experience ln management of extension programs.



- 66 -
ANN= I
Page 15 of 18
48.         Since EIDUs will be a pivotal point for extension activities, it is
important that they have adequate office accommodation and facllities for
farmers' visits, meetings, exhibitions, audio-virual programs, etc. The EIDU in
Nubariya region will, in addition, need resident kal accommodation as no private
or government accommodation is available at the sites.
B. Management. Programmingt Planning and Lmplementation
49.         overall responsibility for implementing the technical aspects will
rest with the Technical Deputy Director of the Project Management Department
(PMD). The Director General of the ARC, and the First Undersecretary (Extensionj
of MALR, together with the Undersecretary Extension, will work in close accord
with the Deputy Director. The Project Management Department will allocate funds,
monitor progress and receive reports from the pilot MRESs.
50.         Among SMSs, one will be chosen to serve as the team leader, and the
team will establish regular liaison and communication with the local
universities, private traders, machinery suppliers/operators and selected
farmers, as well as rural financing institutions in the region. Each specialist
will be responsible for identifying new technologies emanating from various
sources including government institutions, private sector and foreign
institutions. The SMSs will have the overall responsibility for formulating and
implementing technology transfer programs in their respective fields, in
collaboration with the Team Leaders and the TTSs in the EIDUs. Each SMS will
also train TTSs in their concerned fields. Administratively and operationally,
the SNSs will be directly linked to the Director General of the ARC through the
Director of the regional RRES. Team leaders will have direct access to the
Project Management Department (the Technical Deputy Director in the PMD)
concerning fund allocations, procurement, etc. The team leader will also manage
accounts and disburse funds to the EIDU for on-farm demonstration and other
activities. Each RRES will have needed support staff comprising of typist (1),
office help (1), technical assistants (2), accountant (1), and drivers (4).
51.         The extensionist will be responsible for managing the EIDU and the
field program, as well as preparing and handling audio-visual materials. He will
be the leader of the group.   TTSs in the four specialized fields will be
responsible  for  planning  and  implementing  programs  including  on-farm
demonstration with the support of specialists in the RRESs. Also VEWs will be
involved, and the participation of farmers, credit officers, custom hire
operators, machinery dealers/manufacturers and traders, including input
suppliers, will be mobilized.   Each unit will function as a part of the
governorate service under the overall direction of the Undersecretary of
Extension in MALR. Each EIDU will have support staff comprising of typist (1),
watchman (2), technical assistant (1), office help (1), and drivers (2). The
project will provide some funds to develop the market intelligence data needed
to be dispatched to pilot RRESs, who, in turn, would disseminate the information
promptly through the concerned TTSs, village banks and other channels, to
producers and investors.



- 67 -
Page 16 of 18
52.         The key element of program planning will be that it should be demand
driven and decentralized to the level of farming communities -- the
beneficiaries.    Accordingly,  the  TTS  in  each  EIDU  working  closely  in
collaboration with the VEW will identify specific problems and needs of the
farmers. Based on this information, the TTS in collaboration with SMS in the
RRES will formulate a three-year rolling program, which will be flexible to
accommodate changes in the light of experience gained. In this exercise, credit
officers at the district and village levels and private sector will be involved.
Farmers participation will be ensured through field visits and meetings in the
EIDUs. Annual work plan with definite targets will be followed. The TTSs will
also provide feedback to research or other organizations (such as import
suppliers, university faculty concerned, etc.) regarding problems which need
further investigation. The Team leader will be responsible for compiling the
program for the entire region. Before submitting the program to PMD, it will be
discussed in a workshop in which the extension staff, TTSs, SMSs farmers'
representatives, etc., will participate. Approved programs for the three regions
will be printed and distributed to all EIDUs.
53.         The project would promote collaborative team work among technical
services, especially in extension and rural banking. In order for rural banking
to respond to the dynamic and changing investment needs of agriculture, the
project would strengthen PBDAC's as well as other participating banks' capacity
in rural enterprise and on-farm-level investment. Different and multiple units
which are currently performing the same functions in this area within the PBDAC
would be consolidated in currently serving PBDAC in this area will be assemble
under two departments: (i) Agro-Related; and (ii) On-Farm Investment and
Promotion departments. Qualified and senior staff will be posted to head each
of these departments, and other key technical staff will be identified to assume
senior positions. Appropriate international and local experts would be financed
to support these departments in their works. These two departments would advise
the BDACs, branch banks, and village banks to provide specialized service to
their clients, and would foster among the rural banking, and technical research
and extension staff a collaborative work approach. A roster of consultants will
be compiled and made available to the participating banks as well as to
interested investors.   The training programs for upgrading staff skills in
project appraisal would be open to the staff of the PBDAC network as well as the
participating banks.
54.         The project's disbursement procedures for on-lending purposes would
follow the disbursement practices established under the Second Agricultural
Development Project (Ln. 2561-BGT). Accordingly, PBDAC and PBs would avail
proceeds of the project on the basis of a cash or credit option. In the case of
cash option, PBDAC would provide foreign exchange to qualified importers and
traders against an equivalent amount of local currency. Under the credit option,
PBDAC and PBs would provide credit to traders and importers by opening letters
of credit. The local funds generated through direct sale of foreign exchange
would be used for on-lending purposes to producers and investors. The project
would simplify PBDAC procedures concerning opening of letters of credit,
collateral, and down payment.   The on-going Second Agricultural Development
Project has already enabled the establishment of 83 local dealerships for
machinery manufacturers.   The project would enlarge the number of suppliers
further and promote strong competition and increased range of choices of
equipment for custome-hire operators and other beneficiaries.

ARMB REPUBLIC OF EGYPT
AGRICULTURAL MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Proposed Terms of Reference for ARro-Industrv Advisor
Objectives
1.       The key role of the advisor is to assist PBDAC to: (i) establish an
appropriate structure and mechanism for diversifying and expanding its lending
portfolio in small and medium scale agro-industry lending; and (ii) improve its
capability to carry out the technical evaluation of agro-industrial loan
applications and to provide an improved technical advisory service to clients.
Outmuts and Tasks
-   prepare a series of agro-industrial investment prospectus with
models of "workable' sub-projects;
-   prepare the agro-industrial section of a Project Proposal Manual
for use by Bank credit officers;
-   establish an information database of institutions, equipment,
packaging, etc;
-   assist PBDAC in preparing a roster of local and foreign consultant
specialists in agro-industry and rural enterprises;
-   assist PBDAC in preparing a list of relevant training, institutes
both locally and abroad and in developing training curriculum for
agro-industrial and rural enterprise lending;
-   assist in formulation and execution of the PBDAC's staff training
in agro-industry lending;
-   network with the SMSs in agro-industries in the Regional Research
and Extension Stations (RRESs) and assist them in formulation of
their training program and provide general guidance to Technology
Transfer   Specialists   (TTSs)   in   presenting   agro-industry
opportunities to potential investors; and
-   assist PBDAC and local consultants in the design and presentation
of agro-industrial investment promotion investment campaigns.
2.       In addition, the advisor would be required to liaise closely with
institutions in Egypt (ARC, RRESs Universities) and in other countries to
identify product and process opportunities and expertise.



- 72 -
ANNEX 
Appendix 1
Page 2 of 2
3.       The  advisor would  need to work closely with  senior PBDAC  and
participating banks' officials, and specialists in economics, environment,
marketing, etc.
Person Profile
4.       The ideal candidate would:
(a) Have a broad knowledge of food and crop processing in as many as
possible of the following areas:
-   animal feeds
-   milling
-   oil extraction
-   fruit and vegetable processing
-   dairy products
-   post harvest fresh fruit and vegetable handling
-   high value crops (medicinal plants, essential oils, etc.)
-   packaging
(b) Have a good institutional knowledge of sources of expertise and
information.
(c) Be able to work in a flexible way, to think laterally and have the
ability to motivate people from different disciplines in order to
form teams.
(d) Ability to communicate in Arabic desirable.
